Sects and Disagreements

The Mu'tazili Doctrine of Human Power and Their Internal Disputes
States the Mu'tazili view and two points of disagreement among them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view that human power alone produces acts, and the two key debates among their scholars: the role of motive and the nature of our knowledge of human agency.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila agreed on independent human power but split on motive necessity and knowledge type.

Among the theological views on human agency, the Mu'tazila held that the slave's power is the sole effective cause of his actions. However, this school was not monolithic; its scholars debated two crucial points: whether the power's effect requires a motive, and whether our knowledge that we are the agents of our acts is innate or acquired. This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah reports these internal disagreements without taking sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
Read the opening sentence: the fourth view is that the slave's power independently causes the act. This is the Mu'tazili position.
2
Understand the first dispute: motive dependence
Notice that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri wavered: he denied motive dependence in divine matters but affirmed it in human actions. Most Mu'tazila did not require a motive.
The sub-question: if motive is needed, does the act become necessary or merely more worthy? Abu al-Husayn said necessary; Mahmud al-Khwarizmi said more worthy.
3
Understand the second dispute: knowledge type
The majority held knowledge of being the originator of acts is theoretical (acquired), while Abu al-Husayn and Mahmud said it is necessary (innate).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these debates matter: they affect how we understand human responsibility and divine predestination within Mu'tazili theology.
The passage illustrates that even within a school, there can be significant internal diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الرابع : إن المؤثر في حصول الفعل هو قدرة العبد على سبيل الاستقلال . وهذا هو قول المعتزلة ثم هؤلاء اختلفوا في مواضع : فالأول : إن تأثير القدرة في حصول الفعل. هل هو موقوف على حصول ؟ أما « أبو الحسين ، فقد اضطرب قوله فيه . فكلما تكلم مع الداعي في قولهم : لم خصص الله إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين ، دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ قال : ( الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي ، وكلما تكلم في سائر المسائل مع أصحابه . قال : و الفعل يتوقف على الداعي ، وأن الرجحان من غير المرجح : باطل في بدائه العقول ) . وأما مشايخ المعتزلة فأكثرهم [ لا يوقفون ] حصول الفعل على الداعي . ثم إذا قلنا : إن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي . فهل يصير الفعل واجب الوقوع عند حصول الداعية الخالصة ؟ فالأليق بكلام و أبي الحسين ، أنه يسلم الوجوب . وقال صاحبه ( محمود الخوارزمي ) : ( إن الفعل عند حصول الداعي يترقى من حد التساوي ، ولكن لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب ، بل يصير أولى بالوقوع » . والثاني : إن العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه : علم ضروري أو نظري ؟ فالجمهور من مشايخ المعتزلة اتفقوا على أنه علم نظري . وأما « أبو الحسين ، فانه يزعم : أنه علم ضروري . وهو الذي اختاره و محمود الخوارزمي ) .
And the fourth view: that the effect in the occurrence of the act is the slave's power independently. This is the saying of the Mu'tazila. Then these disagreed in places: the first: whether the effect of power in the occurrence of the act is dependent on the occurrence of the motive? As for Abu al-Husayn, his statement on it wavered. Whenever he spoke about the motive in their saying: 'Why did Allah specify the origination of the world at a particular time, not before or after?' he said: 'The act is not dependent on the motive.' And whenever he spoke in other issues with his companions, he said: 'The act is dependent on the motive, and that preponderance without a preponderator is false in the beginnings of intellects.' And as for the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila, most of them do not make the occurrence of the act dependent on the motive. Then if we say: the act is dependent on the motive, then does the act become necessary of occurrence when the pure motive is obtained? The more fitting with the speech of Abu al-Husayn is that he concedes necessity. And his companion Mahmud al-Khwarizmi said: 'The act at the occurrence of the motive ascends from the level of equivalence but does not reach the level of necessity, rather it becomes more worthy of occurrence.' And the second: whether the knowledge that the slave is an originator of his own acts is necessary knowledge or theoretical? The majority of the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila agreed that it is theoretical knowledge. As for Abu al-Husayn, he claims that it is necessary knowledge, and this is what he and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi chose.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth view in the series of doctrines on human agency, namely that the effect in producing the act is entirely the slave's power, independent of any other cause. This is ascribed to the Mu'tazila. The author then notes that the Mu'tazila disagreed among themselves on several points. The first point of disagreement concerns whether the power's effect in producing the act is dependent on the presence of a motive (da'i). The author reports that Abu al-Husayn (al-Basri) was inconsistent: when discussing divine acts he denied dependence on motive, but in other contexts he affirmed it and held that preponderance without a preponderator is rationally impossible. Most Mu'tazili shaykhs, however, do not make the act dependent on motive. Then the author poses a further question: if we do hold that the act depends on motive, does the act become necessary (wajib) when the pure motive is present? According to Abu al-Husayn, the act would be necessitated. But Mahmud al-Khwarizmi argued that it only becomes more worthy (awla) of occurrence, not absolutely necessary. The second point of disagreement concerns whether our knowledge that the slave is the originator of his own actions is necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari). The majority of Mu'tazili shaykhs said it is theoretical, while Abu al-Husayn and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi held it is necessary. The author here merely reports these positions without endorsing any.

Key Terms
قدرة
داعي
وجوب
أولى بالوقوع
علم ضروري
علم نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Mu'tazila held identical views on human power.
Correction: This passage shows significant internal disagreements, for example on the role of motive and the epistemic status of agency knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Mahmud al-Khwarizmi, when the motive (da'i) is present, the act becomes:
A) Necessary (wajib)
B) More worthy (awla) of occurrence
C) Impossible (mumtani')
D) Indifferent (mubah)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila held that human power alone produces acts, but they disagreed internally on whether a motive is required and whether our knowledge of agency is innate or acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you feel an inner motive pushing you, or do you feel you choose freely? The Mu'tazila debated whether the act depends on the motive. Think of a recent decision you made: what was the strongest motive, and did you feel you could have gone against it? How does this make you examine the reality of your free will? Will you take a moment today to consciously choose an action that goes against a strong whim, just to test your own independence?
?
The Mu'tazila disagreed on whether our knowledge of being the originator of our acts is innate or acquired. Reflect on your own inner conviction: when you sin, do you instinctively blame yourself, or do you reason that Allah created that action? If the knowledge is innate, why do you sometimes feel trapped by circumstances? What would it mean for your accountability if you truly believed your agency is a necessary knowledge – that you cannot deny it?
?
If acts become necessary when the pure motive is present, then do you ever feel that your worship is coerced by fear of hell or desire of paradise? Mahmud al-Khwarizmi said it becomes 'more worthy' but not necessary. Does that allow for true love of Allah? Examine your worship: is it driven by a motive that leaves you no choice, or do you freely choose to worship? How can you cultivate a state where your motive is pure love, yet you feel the freedom to turn away? And if you feel that freedom, what does that say about your sincerity?

Mu'tazila's First Objection: Jabr Implies God as Necessary Agent
Mu'tazila present their objection that jabr implies God is a necessary agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the Ash'ari concept of jabr (determinism) logically entails that God is an agent by essence (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not a voluntary agent.

Core Idea
If jabr is true for humans, it logically extends to God, making Him a necessary agent.

The Mu'tazila launch a sharp critique against the Ash'ari doctrine of jabr. They argue that the very reasoning used to affirm determinism in human actions—the necessity of act when power and motive are present—cannot be confined to humans. When applied to God's action, it leads to the conclusion that God is a necessary agent, which contradicts the Ash'ari commitment to God's voluntary agency. This passage presents the core of their objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the first half of the passage to understand the Mu'tazilite assertion that jabr only makes sense if God is a necessary agent.
Focus on the definition of jabr given by the Jabriyya.
2
Understand the definition of jabr
Identify the condition for jabr: when power and pure motive coincide, the act is necessary; when absent, impossible.
This is the key premise the Mu'tazila will apply to God.
3
Apply the logic to God
See how the Mu'tazila apply the same reasoning to divine agency, arguing that the conditions for God's action lead to either pre-eternity or infinite regress.
Notice the dilemma: either the conditions are eternal (necessitating the world) or they are not (leading to regress).
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument forces the conclusion that God is a necessary agent, not a voluntary one.
The Mu'tazila claim that the logic of jabr cannot be limited to humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : لقائل من المعتزلة أن يقول : الجبر لا يتأتى إلا مع القول بكون الباري موجباً بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار . لأن حاصل كلام الجبرية هو : أن عند حصول القدرة والداعية الخالصة يجب الفعل ، وعند فقدان هذا المجموع يمتنع الفعل . وهذا هو القول بالجبر . إلا أن هذا الكلام بعينه وارد في فاعلية الله . لأن كل ما لا بد منه في فاعلية الله تعالى . إن كان حاصلا في الأزل ، وجب حصول العالم معه، وإن لم يكن أزلياً ، افتقر حدوثه إلى سبب آخر ، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى الصفات الأزلية . وحينئذ يعود هذا السؤال . قالوا : فثبت : أن دليلكم في إثبات الجبر، إن تم وكمل فهو ، يوجب القول بالجبر شاهداً أو غائباً . وذلك يوجب القول بقدم العالم ، وكونه تعالى موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلا بالاختيار . كما هو قول الفلاسفة وأما إن قلنا : إن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعية ، وإن توقف عليها لكن صدور الفعل عند حصول الداعية غير واجب . فهذا قول المعتزلة . وبطل القول بالجبر في الشاهد [ والغائب (۱) ] فثبت : أن الحق . إما القول بالجبر شاهداً وغائباً ، كما هو قول الفلاسفة . أو القول بنفيه شاهداً وغائباً ، كما هو قول المعتزلة . فأما القول بإثباته في الشاهد ، ونفيه في الغائب ، كما هو قولكم فكلام متناقض فهذا هو الكلام الأقوى من جانبهم .
The Second Investigation: A speaker from the Mu'tazila may say: Jabr (determinism) is only realized with the statement that the Creator is an agent by essence (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not a voluntary agent (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār). Because the gist of the speech of the Jabriyya is: when power and pure motive (dā‘iya khāliṣa) are present, the act is necessary, and when this combination is absent, the act is impossible. This is the statement of jabr. However, this very same speech applies to God’s agency (fā‘iliyya). Because everything necessary for God’s agency — if it was present from eternity (azal), then the existence of the world would be necessary with it; and if it was not eternal, then its origination would need another cause, and there is no infinite regress but it ends with the eternal attributes. Then this question returns. They said: It is established that your proof for jabr, if it is complete and perfect, necessitates the statement of jabr in the seen (shāhid) or the unseen (ghā’ib). And that necessitates the statement of the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-‘ālam) and that He, exalted, is an agent by essence, not a voluntary agent, as is the statement of the philosophers. And if we say that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent (qādir) does not depend on the motive, or even if it depends on it but the occurrence of the act upon the presence of the motive is not necessary — this is the statement of the Mu‘tazila, and the statement of jabr is invalidated in the seen and the unseen. So it is established that the truth is either the statement of jabr in the seen and unseen, as is the statement of the philosophers, or the statement of its negation in the seen and unseen, as is the statement of the Mu‘tazila. As for the statement of affirming it in the seen and negating it in the unseen, as is your statement, it is contradictory speech. So this is the strongest speech from their side.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an objection from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position on jabr (determinism). The Mu'tazila begin by arguing that the concept of jabr logically entails affirming that God is an agent by essence (necessarily producing effects), not a voluntary agent with choice. They analyze the typical jabr argument: when power and pure motive coincide, the act is necessary; when they are absent, the act is impossible. They then apply this same logic to God's agency: either the conditions for God's action are eternal, thus the world is co-eternal (philosophers' view), or they are not eternal, leading to an infinite regress that ends only in eternal attributes, bringing back the same question. Thus, they claim, the Ash'ari proof for jabr would force one to affirm jabr both in the seen (human actions) and the unseen (divine actions), leading to the philosopher's position of a necessary agent and a pre-eternal world. Alternatively, one could negate jabr entirely in both realms, as the Mu'tazila do. The Ash'ari position—affirming jabr in the seen but denying it in the unseen—is labeled as contradictory. The author concludes this section by acknowledging this as the strongest argument from the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi al-dhāt)
فاعل بالاختيار (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār)
قدرة (qudra)
داعية (dā‘iya)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā’ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That jabr is only about human actions and has no implications for divine agency.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that the same logic used to affirm jabr in humans, when consistently applied, forces one to conclude that God is a necessary agent, which contradicts the Ash'ari position of voluntary agency.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument, what does the Ash'ari concept of jabr in humans imply about God's agency?
A) God is a voluntary agent
B) God is an agent by necessity (mūjib bi al-dhāt)
C) God has no agency
D) God's agency is independent of conditions

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that jabr cannot be confined to humans; it logically extends to God, making Him a necessary agent, not a voluntary one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed your sins on 'fate' or 'God's decree' to avoid feeling responsible? How does the Mu'tazila's argument that jabr leads to absurdity about God make you reconsider the comfort of determinism?
?
If you truly believed God is a voluntary agent who could have chosen otherwise, how would that change the way you pray or ask for forgiveness? Are you treating God as if He were bound by a set of rules?
?
The Mu'tazila force us to follow logic to its conclusion—even if it challenges comfortable beliefs. Is there an area of your faith where you avoid thinking through the implications because they might be uncomfortable? Will you confront that today?

Sects and Disagreements

The Dilemma: Either Jabr in Both Realms or None
Mu'tazila present the dilemma and conclude Ash'ari position is contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite conclusion that the truth is either jabr in both seen and unseen realms (as philosophers hold) or its negation in both (as Mu'tazila hold), and that the Ash'ari middle position is contradictory.

Core Idea
Consistency demands jabr either everywhere or nowhere; Ash'ari's split position is contradictory.

In this continuation, the Mu'tazila draw out the implications of the previous argument. They conclude that the Ash'ari proof for jabr, if valid, would force one to affirm jabr both in the human realm (shāhid) and in the divine realm (ghā'ib). This would lead to the philosopher's position of a necessary agent and a pre-eternal world. Conversely, if one denies that logic (by saying act does not depend on motive), then jabr is negated in both realms. The Ash'ari position—affirming jabr in humans but denying it in God—is thus contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion of the objection
Read the second part of the passage to see the Mu'tazilite conclusion.
Pay attention to the two options presented.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Identify the first option: affirm jabr in both realms leading to philosophers' view. Second option: negate jabr in both realms leading to Mu'tazilite view.
The Mu'tazila present these as the only consistent positions.
3
Understand why the Ash'ari position is contradictory
See why affirming jabr in humans but denying it in God is considered logically contradictory.
The Mu'tazila label it 'kalam mutanāqiḍ' (contradictory speech).
4
Appreciate the strength of the argument
Note that the author of the original text acknowledges this as the strongest argument from the Mu'tazila.
This sets up the subsequent Ash'ari response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : لقائل من المعتزلة أن يقول : الجبر لا يتأتى إلا مع القول بكون الباري موجباً بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار . لأن حاصل كلام الجبرية هو : أن عند حصول القدرة والداعية الخالصة يجب الفعل ، وعند فقدان هذا المجموع يمتنع الفعل . وهذا هو القول بالجبر . إلا أن هذا الكلام بعينه وارد في فاعلية الله . لأن كل ما لا بد منه في فاعلية الله تعالى . إن كان حاصلا في الأزل ، وجب حصول العالم معه، وإن لم يكن أزلياً ، افتقر حدوثه إلى سبب آخر ، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى الصفات الأزلية . وحينئذ يعود هذا السؤال . قالوا : فثبت : أن دليلكم في إثبات الجبر، إن تم وكمل فهو ، يوجب القول بالجبر شاهداً أو غائباً . وذلك يوجب القول بقدم العالم ، وكونه تعالى موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلا بالاختيار . كما هو قول الفلاسفة وأما إن قلنا : إن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعية ، وإن توقف عليها لكن صدور الفعل عند حصول الداعية غير واجب . فهذا قول المعتزلة . وبطل القول بالجبر في الشاهد [ والغائب (۱) ] فثبت : أن الحق . إما القول بالجبر شاهداً وغائباً ، كما هو قول الفلاسفة . أو القول بنفيه شاهداً وغائباً ، كما هو قول المعتزلة . فأما القول بإثباته في الشاهد ، ونفيه في الغائب ، كما هو قولكم فكلام متناقض فهذا هو الكلام الأقوى من جانبهم .
The Second Investigation: A speaker from the Mu'tazila may say: Jabr (determinism) is only realized with the statement that the Creator is an agent by essence (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not a voluntary agent (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār). Because the gist of the speech of the Jabriyya is: when power and pure motive (dā‘iya khāliṣa) are present, the act is necessary, and when this combination is absent, the act is impossible. This is the statement of jabr. However, this very same speech applies to God’s agency (fā‘iliyya). Because everything necessary for God’s agency — if it was present from eternity (azal), then the existence of the world would be necessary with it; and if it was not eternal, then its origination would need another cause, and there is no infinite regress but it ends with the eternal attributes. Then this question returns. They said: It is established that your proof for jabr, if it is complete and perfect, necessitates the statement of jabr in the seen (shāhid) or the unseen (ghā’ib). And that necessitates the statement of the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-‘ālam) and that He, exalted, is an agent by essence, not a voluntary agent, as is the statement of the philosophers. And if we say that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent (qādir) does not depend on the motive, or even if it depends on it but the occurrence of the act upon the presence of the motive is not necessary — this is the statement of the Mu‘tazila, and the statement of jabr is invalidated in the seen and the unseen. So it is established that the truth is either the statement of jabr in the seen and unseen, as is the statement of the philosophers, or the statement of its negation in the seen and unseen, as is the statement of the Mu‘tazila. As for the statement of affirming it in the seen and negating it in the unseen, as is your statement, it is contradictory speech. So this is the strongest speech from their side.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an objection from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position on jabr (determinism). The Mu'tazila begin by arguing that the concept of jabr logically entails affirming that God is an agent by essence (necessarily producing effects), not a voluntary agent with choice. They analyze the typical jabr argument: when power and pure motive coincide, the act is necessary; when they are absent, the act is impossible. They then apply this same logic to God's agency: either the conditions for God's action are eternal, thus the world is co-eternal (philosophers' view), or they are not eternal, leading to an infinite regress that ends only in eternal attributes, bringing back the same question. Thus, they claim, the Ash'ari proof for jabr would force one to affirm jabr both in the seen (human actions) and the unseen (divine actions), leading to the philosopher's position of a necessary agent and a pre-eternal world. Alternatively, one could negate jabr entirely in both realms, as the Mu'tazila do. The Ash'ari position—affirming jabr in the seen but denying it in the unseen—is labeled as contradictory. The author concludes this section by acknowledging this as the strongest argument from the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā’ib)
قدم العالم (qidam al-‘ālam)
فلاسفة (falāsifa)
معتزلة (Mu‘tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari position on jabr is logically consistent and can be confined to humans.
Correction: The Mu'tazila demonstrate that consistency requires either affirming jabr in both realms or negating it in both; the Ash'ari middle position is logically contradictory.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazilite argument claim about the Ash'ari position of affirming jabr in the seen but denying it in the unseen?
A) It is the most rational position
B) It is the statement of the philosophers
C) It is contradictory (kalam mutanāqiḍ)
D) It is the same as the Mu'tazilite position

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila force a dilemma: either jabr applies consistently in both human and divine realms, leading to necessary agency and pre-eternity, or it is negated in both. The Ash'ari split position is logically contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that you cannot hold inconsistent beliefs about God and humans. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold contradictory principles because they are comfortable? What would it cost you to bring them into alignment with the truth?
?
If the Ash'ari position is truly contradictory, then what does that mean for you? Are you willing to abandon a middle-ground position that feels safe for a more rigorous consistency, even if it forces you to choose a harder path?
?
The philosophers' position (full jabr) leads to a God who is not a voluntary agent but a necessary cause. Can you personally accept that view? If not, then your rejection of jabr in the unseen must also affect how you view jabr in humans. How will you reconcile this in your daily reliance on God's will?

Ash'ari Theory of Four Accidents and Mu'tazili Contrast
Details the Ash'ari theory of four simultaneous accidents and contrasts the Mu'tazili view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari theory of four simultaneous accidents in human action and the contrasting Mu'tazili view regarding the efficacy of created power.

Core Idea
God creates all actions; human power is created but ineffective.

The Ash'ari school developed a detailed occasionalist account of human action. When you move a pen, God creates multiple accidents simultaneously—including your will, ability, and the movement itself. This passage presents that theory and contrasts it with the Mu'tazili view that the created ability has an effect. Let's unpack the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the four accidents listed and the contrast between schools.
The passage uses a pen‐moving example to illustrate the theory.
2
Identify the four accidents
List the four accidents mentioned: will, ability, hand movement, pen movement. Understand that they are simultaneous with no causal relation.
The Ash'ari view denies that any accident causes another.
3
Compare the Mu'tazili position
Note that the Mu'tazila say the person acts with the created ability, implying efficacy. Also note the minority Ash'ari view that the ability has some effect.
The majority Ash'ari considered the minority view repugnant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أفعال الناس فهم فيها مختلفون . مذهب أكثرهم جمهور الأشعرية - : أن عند تحريك هذا القلم . خلق الله أربعة أعراض ، ليس منها عرض مسببا للآخر . بل هي منقارنة في الوجود لا غير . العرض الأول : إرادتي أن أحرك القلم . والعرض الثاني : قدرتي على تحريكه . والعرض الثالث : نفس الحركة الإنسانية - أعني حركة اليد - والعرض الرابع : تحرك القلم . لأنهم زعموا : أن الإنسان إذا أراد شيئا ففعله - بزعمه - فقد خلقت له الإرادة ، وخلقت له القدرة على فعل ما أراد ، وخلق له الفعل . لأنه يفعل بالقدرة المخلوقة فيه ، ولا أثر لها في الفصل أما المعتزلة : فقالوا إنه يفعل بالقدرة المخلوقة فيه. وبعض الأشعرية قال : « للقدرة المخلوقة في الفعل أثر ما ، ولها به تعلق ، ولكن أكثرهم استشنعوا ذلك . وهذه الإرادة المخلوقة - على زعم - كلهم - والقدرة المخلوقة . وكذلك الفعل المخلوق عند بعضهم . كل ذلك أعراض لا بقاء :
As for the actions of people, they differ regarding them. The madhhab of most of them, the majority of the Ash'aris, is that when moving this pen, God creates four accidents, none of which is a cause of the other. Rather, they are simultaneous in existence only. The first accident: my will to move the pen. The second accident: my ability to move it. The third accident: the human movement itself – I mean the movement of the hand – and the fourth accident: the movement of the pen. Because they claimed that when a person wills something and then does it – in their claim – his will is created for him, and his ability is created for him to do what he willed, and the action is created for him. Because he acts with the created ability in him, and it has no effect on the action. As for the Mu'tazila, they said that he acts with the created ability in him. And some Ash'aris said: 'The created ability has some effect on the action, and it has a relation to it,' but most of them considered that repugnant. And this created will – in their claim – all of them, and the created ability, and likewise the created action according to some of them, all of that are accidents that do not endure:
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a footnote explaining the differing positions on human action. The author describes the Ash'ari view in detail: when a person moves a pen, God creates four distinct accidents simultaneously—will, ability, hand movement, and pen movement—none causing the other. The person does not create his action; God creates it along with the ability, which has no effect. Then the Mu'tazili view: the person acts with that created ability, implying efficacy. Some Ash'aris held a middle view that the created ability has some effect and relation to the action, but this was deemed objectionable by the majority. The note concludes that all these accidents (will, ability, action) are perishable—they do not endure. This is a classic summary of Ash'ari occasionalism and the debate over human agency.

Key Terms
الأشعرية
المعتزلة
أعراض
إرادة
قدرة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny any role for human power
Correction: Ash'aris affirm a created power in the human but deny its causal efficacy. They do not deny the existence of human power, only its independent effect.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view described, how many accidents does God create when a person moves a pen?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view of human action involves God creating four simultaneous accidents with no causal efficacy from the human's created power, while the Mu'tazila affirm efficacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake up tomorrow and choose to pray, will you sense that even your desire to pray was created by God in that moment? How would your gratitude shift if you truly believed your will and ability are God's creation with no independent effect?
?
This passage says God creates your hand's movement and the pen's movement simultaneously—your moving hand does not cause the pen to move. Is your view of causality shaken? Would you still feel responsible for your actions if you knew your power had no causal role?
?
If your ability to obey God is created and ineffective, then every good deed is entirely God’s gift and every sin is your own? But sin exists—does that mean you are still the source of evil? How do you live with the tension between God creating all actions and your own accountability?

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Four Accidents
Sanusi describes the Ash'ari view of four simultaneous accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that human actions involve four simultaneously created accidents, with no causal connection between them.

Core Idea
God creates will, power, movement, and pen motion as separate accidents.

This passage opens the discussion on human actions by presenting the mainstream Ash'ari position. It details the four accidents that God creates when a person moves a pen, stressing that they are not causally linked but merely coexistent. Understanding this sets the stage for contrasting views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the details.
Notice the enumerative style: the four accidents are listed explicitly.
2
Identify the four accidents
List the four accidents in order: will, power, hand movement, pen movement.
The author emphasizes they are simultaneous and not causally related.
3
Understand the role of created power
Note that the human acts by the created power, but that power has no effect on the action. This is the Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition).
This distinguishes Ash'aris from Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أفعال الناس فهم فيها مختلفون . مذهب أكثرهم جمهور الأشعرية - : أن عند تحريك هذا القلم . خلق الله أربعة أعراض ، ليس منها عرض مسببا للآخر . بل هي منقارنة في الوجود لا غير . العرض الأول : إرادتي أن أحرك القلم . والعرض الثاني : قدرتي على تحريكه . والعرض الثالث : نفس الحركة الإنسانية - أعني حركة اليد - والعرض الرابع : تحرك القلم . لأنهم زعموا : أن الإنسان إذا أراد شيئا ففعله - بزعمه - فقد خلقت له الإرادة ، وخلقت له القدرة على فعل ما أراد ، وخلق له الفعل . لأنه يفعل بالقدرة المخلوقة فيه ، ولا أثر لها في الفصل
أما المعتزلة : فقالوا إنه يفعل بالقدرة المخلوقة فيه. وبعض الأشعرية قال : « للقدرة المخلوقة في الفعل أثر ما ، ولها به تعلق ، ولكن أكثرهم استشنعوا ذلك . وهذه الإرادة المخلوقة - على
زعم - كلهم - والقدرة المخلوقة . وكذلك الفعل المخلوق عند بعضهم . كل ذلك أعراض لا بقاء :
لها. وإنما الله يخلق في هذا القلم تحركا بعد تحرك . هكذا دائها طالما القلم متحركا . فإذا سكن . لم يكن حتى خلق الله فيه أيضا سكونا ، ولا يبرح يخلق فيه سكونا بعد سكون ، طالما القلم ساكنا ففي كل أن من تلك الآنات - أعني الأزمنة المنفردة - يخلق الله عرضا في جميع أشخاص الموجودات . من ملك وفلك وغيرهما ، هكذا دائما في كل حين . وقالوا : إن هذا هو الإيمان الحقيقي بإن الله فاعل. ومن لم يعتقد أن هكذا يفعل الله ، فقد جحد كون الله فاعلا - على رأيهم : [ دلالة الحائرين ص ٢٠٨ ] .
ذكر هذا النص : موسى بن ميمون ، وعلق عليه بقوله : في مثل هذه الاعتقادات يقال عندي وعند كل ذي عقل : ( أم أنتم تخدعونه، كما يخدع إنسان ، ؟ [ ٩:١٢ ] إذ هذا هو عين
الخدعة حقيقة .
As for the actions of people, they differ concerning them. The school of the majority, the Ash'aris, is that upon moving this pen, God creates four accidents, none of which is a cause for the other, but they are simultaneous in existence, no more. The first accident: my will to move the pen. The second accident: my power to move it. The third accident: the human movement itself—I mean the movement of the hand. The fourth accident: the movement of the pen. Because they claimed that when a human wills something and then does it—according to his claim—will is created for him, power is created for him to do what he willed, and the action is created for him. Because he acts by the created power in him, and it has no effect on the action. As for the Mu'tazila, they said: He acts by the created power in him. And some Ash'aris said: 'The created power has some effect on the action and has a connection with it, but the majority found this repugnant.' These created will—according to their claim—all of them, and the created power, and likewise the created action according to some, all are accidents that do not endure. And indeed God creates in this pen movement after movement, thus always as long as the pen is moving. And when it becomes still, it does not become still until God also creates stillness in it, and He continues creating stillness after stillness as long as the pen is still. So in every moment of those instants—I mean the individual times—God creates an accident in all individuals of existing things, from angel and sphere and others, thus always at every time. And they said: This is the true belief that God is agent. And whoever does not believe that God acts thus has denied that God is an agent—according to their view. [Dalalat al-Ha'irin p. 208.] Moses ben Maimon mentioned this text and commented on it saying: Regarding such beliefs, it is said by me and by every rational person: 'Or do you deceive him as a human is deceived?' [9:12] For this is the very essence of deception.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a footnote that details the positions of different theological schools on human actions. It begins by stating that people differ on this issue. The majority of the Ash'aris hold that when a person moves a pen, God creates four accidents simultaneously without causal connection: (1) the will to move, (2) the power to move, (3) the movement of the hand, and (4) the movement of the pen. The human acts by the created power but that power has no effect on the action. The Mu'tazila hold that the human acts by the created power (i.e., the power is efficacious). Some Ash'aris concede that the created power has some effect and connection, but the majority find this repugnant. The passage then explains that according to the Ash'ari theory, all these accidents (will, power, action) are non-enduring, and God continuously creates accidents in all existents at every moment. This is presented as the true belief that God is the sole agent. Finally, the author quotes Moses Maimonides, who criticizes this view as a form of deception, comparing it to trying to deceive God.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
إرادة (will)
قدرة (power)
فعل (action)
أشعرية (Ash'aris)
مخلوقة (created)
أثر (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Ash'ari view denies any role for human agency, equating it with direct divine action.
Correction: The passage shows that actions are attributed to humans in a secondary sense (kasb); God creates the accidents in the human, but the human is still said to 'act' by the created power, even if it has no effect.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position described, how many accidents are created when moving a pen?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris teach that God creates four simultaneous accidents for each human action, with no causal relation between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt proud of your own ability to make decisions or act? How does knowing that God creates even your will and power—with no effect from you—challenge your sense of ownership over your actions? What should change in how you attribute success or failure?
?
Think of a moment today when you moved your hand or spoke a word. Do you believe that you were the true agent, or that God created the movement as a direct accident? If the latter, how does that affect your reliance on God and your awareness of His constant creation in every moment?
?
When you face a difficult choice and struggle to decide, whom do you turn to? If your will is itself created by God, does it make sense to panic about making the 'wrong' decision? How can this teaching free you from anxiety and deepen your trust in Allah's orchestration of all events?

Mu'tazili and Moderate Ash'ari Views on Created Power
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazili and minority Ash'ari views on created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting positions of the Mu'tazila and a minority of Ash'aris on whether the created power has any effect on the action.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila affirm efficacy of created power; majority Ash'aris deny it.

After presenting the mainstream Ash'ari view, the author turns to the Mu'tazili school and a dissenting opinion within Ash'arism. This brief but important passage clarifies the spectrum of views on human agency within Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation to understand the two positions presented.
This is a direct comparison between two schools.
2
Compare the two views
Contrast the Mu'tazili claim (the person acts by the created power) with the minority Ash'ari claim (the created power has some effect and connection).
Note that the majority of Ash'aris found the idea of the power having effect repugnant.

Continuous Creation and Maimonides' Critique
Sanusi explains Ash'ari occasionalism and quotes Maimonides' criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine of continual creation of accidents and Maimonides' critique that it amounts to deception.

Core Idea
God creates accidents at every instant; Maimonides calls this belief deceptive.

This final part of the passage expands the Ash'ari view to a universal theory: God creates and recreates accidents in all things at every moment. The author then quotes Moses Maimonides, who harshly criticizes this worldview as intellectually dishonest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on continuous creation
Read the Arabic describing how God creates movement after movement, stillness after stillness, at every instant.
This is the Ash'ari occasionalist doctrine applied to all existents.
2
Understand the Ash'ari claim of true belief
Notice that the Ash'aris present this as 'true belief' in God as agent, and that denying it amounts to denying God's agency.
3
Examine Maimonides' critique
Read Maimonides' comment accusing this view of being a deception, comparing it to trying to deceive God.
The quote is from the Quran (9:12) adapted to the context.

As for the actions of people, they differ concerning them. The school of the majority, the Ash'aris, is that upon moving this pen, God creates four accidents, none of which is a cause for the other, but they are simultaneous in existence, no more. The first accident: my will to move the pen. The second accident: my power to move it. The third accident: the human movement itself—I mean the movement of the hand. The fourth accident: the movement of the pen. Because they claimed that when a human wills something and then does it—according to his claim—will is created for him, power is created for him to do what he willed, and the action is created for him. Because he acts by the created power in him, and it has no effect on the action. As for the Mu'tazila, they said: He acts by the created power in him. And some Ash'aris said: 'The created power has some effect on the action and has a connection with it, but the majority found this repugnant.' These created will—according to their claim—all of them, and the created power, and likewise the created action according to some, all are accidents that do not endure. And indeed God creates in this pen movement after movement, thus always as long as the pen is moving. And when it becomes still, it does not become still until God also creates stillness in it, and He continues creating stillness after stillness as long as the pen is still. So in every moment of those instants—I mean the individual times—God creates an accident in all individuals of existing things, from angel and sphere and others, thus always at every time. And they said: This is the true belief that God is agent. And whoever does not believe that God acts thus has denied that God is an agent—according to their view. [Dalalat al-Ha'irin p. 208.] Moses ben Maimon mentioned this text and commented on it saying: Regarding such beliefs, it is said by me and by every rational person: 'Or do you deceive him as a human is deceived?' [9:12] For this is the very essence of deception.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a footnote that details the positions of different theological schools on human actions. It begins by stating that people differ on this issue. The majority of the Ash'aris hold that when a person moves a pen, God creates four accidents simultaneously without causal connection: (1) the will to move, (2) the power to move, (3) the movement of the hand, and (4) the movement of the pen. The human acts by the created power but that power has no effect on the action. The Mu'tazila hold that the human acts by the created power (i.e., the power is efficacious). Some Ash'aris concede that the created power has some effect and connection, but the majority find this repugnant. The passage then explains that according to the Ash'ari theory, all these accidents (will, power, action) are non-enduring, and God continuously creates accidents in all existents at every moment. This is presented as the true belief that God is the sole agent. Finally, the author quotes Moses Maimonides, who criticizes this view as a form of deception, comparing it to trying to deceive God.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
إرادة (will)
قدرة (power)
فعل (action)
أشعرية (Ash'aris)
مخلوقة (created)
إيمان (belief)
فاعل (agent)
خدعة (deception)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Ash'ari view of continuous creation was universally accepted as the correct understanding of divine agency.
Correction: Maimonides' critique shows that it was controversial and considered intellectually dishonest by some rationalist thinkers.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris in this passage, what does God create at every instant in all existing things?
A) Substances
B) Accidents
C) Souls
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of continuous accident creation is presented as the true belief in God's agency, but Maimonides denounces it as a form of deception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single movement of your body—every heartbeat, every blink—is created by God at this very instant? Or have you become comfortable attributing things to 'nature' or 'habit' while claiming to believe in God's omnipotence? How would your daily reliance on God change if you lived with this awareness?
?
Maimonides called this belief a 'deception.' Have you ever felt uncomfortable with the radical implications of God's continuous creation? Do you prefer a God who sets the world in motion and then steps back, because it feels safer or less intrusive? What does that preference reveal about your heart's desire for autonomy?
?
The passage says that whoever does not believe that God acts in this continuous manner has denied God's agency. Examine yourself: in which areas of your life do you still act as if things happen on their own, without God's immediate creative act? Will you repent of that denial today and ask God to make you truly reliant on Him in every moment?

Introduction and First Refutation: Identity and Quiddity
First refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim using identity and quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite principle that like things have like rulings and how the author refutes it using the example of individuals identical in quiddity yet distinct in identity.

Core Idea
Complete quiddity does not guarantee identical rulings—individual identity matters.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that what is valid for one thing is valid for its like. This seems intuitive, but the author argues it fails in many cases. Let's start with the first counterexample, focusing on the distinction between quiddity and individual identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the opening concession and the first example.
The second example is missing due to a lacuna in the manuscript.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite principle
The principle is that what is valid for a thing is valid for its like. The author refutes this by showing that complete quiddity does not imply identical rulings.
3
Understand the first example
Two individuals of the same species are equal in quiddity but one cannot become the other because each has a specific identity. This shows that specification (ta‘ayyun) matters.
4
Apply to divine and human power
The author concludes that the servant's power cannot be like God's power, and refutes the Mu'tazilite idea of a single power between two agents.
5
Note the missing second example
The text jumps from the first to the third example. This lacuna does not affect the overall argument, but it is worth noting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك التعين شرطاً لحصول ذلك الحكم، في أحد الطرفين، أو مانعاً منه في الطرف الآخر؟. سلمنا: أن التعين ساقط الاعتبار، لكن قولكم: ويصح على الشيء ما يصح على مثله، منقوض بصور كثيرة: الأول: إن أفراد النوع الأخير متساوية في تمام الماهية. مع أن تعين هذا يمنع حصوله لذاك، وتعين ذاك يمتنع حصوله لهذا. وإلا لصار هذا عين ذاك، وذاك عين هذا. وكل ذلك محال فمحال أن يكون مقدور العبد مثل المقدور الله تعالى. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يكون مقدور العبد بحيث أن يكون مقدوراً لله تعالى - على مذهب المعتزلة - لأن عندهم مقدور واحد بين قادرين محال. الثالث: إن مشايخ المعتزلة: مذهبهم: أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وإنما يخالف بعضها بعضاً لأجل اختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته الخاصة. فعلى هذا: ذات الله تعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يصح على كل واحد من الذوات كل ما يصح على سائر الذوات. وإلا لزم أن يصح على ذات الله: الحدوث والإمكان، والحاجة إلى المحل، وأن يصح على ذوات المحدثات كونها قديمة، واجبة الوجود. وكل ذلك محال. الرابع: إن صفة الوجود - عندهم - صفة واحدة. فيكون الوجود الحاصل في السواد والبياض: مساوياً للوجود الحاصل في ذات الله. ولم يلزم الاستواء في جميع الأحكام. الخامس: عندهم العرض القائم بمحل يمنع حلوله في المحل الثاني، مع أن مثل ذلك العرض، قد يكون قائماً بالمحل الثاني. فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، قد اختلفت في اللوازم. السادس: إن الإرادتين المعلقتين بالمراد الواحد، على الوجه الواحد: مثلان. ثم إن إحداهما قد تكون في محل، والأخرى لا في محل، والإرادة التي حصلت في المحل يمتنع حصولها لا في محل وبالعكس يمتنع. السابع: العرض الذي لا يبقى: يختص حدوثه بوقت معين. بمعنى أنه حدوثه إلا في الوقت الذي حدث فيه. ثم إن تلك الأعراض متماثلة كالأصوات متماثلة في تمام الماهية فههنا المساواة في تمام الماهية حاصلة، ولم يلزم منه المساواة في كل الأحكام. الثامن: عندهم العبد إذا فعل فعلا، ثم فني. فإنه لا يقدر على إعادته بعد العدم، وقد يقدر على إيجاد مثله. فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، لم تكن متساوية في كل الأحكام. التاسع: الوجود صفة واحدة عندهم. وأيضاً: فعندهم تأثير القادر ليس إلا في تحصيل الوجود. فإن الماهيات - عند مشايخهم - ثابتة في العدم، فيمتنع أن يكون للقادر فيها تأثير. ثم مع هذا، العبد يقدر على تحصيل الوجود لبعض الماهيات دون البعض. العاشر: الجوهر حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى الفاعل، وحال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل والتفاوت بين الذات الواحدة: بحسب وقتين من التفاوت، بين ذاتين متفاوتين. فإذا جاز أن يختلف في الشخص الواحد بحسب وقتين، فيجوز أن يختلف في الشخص الواحد بحسب وقتين.
That specification is a condition for the occurrence of that judgment in one of the two sides, or a prevention from it in the other side? We concede: that specification is disregarded, but your statement: 'And what is valid for a thing is valid for its like' is refuted by many instances: First: The individuals of the final type are equal in complete quiddity, while the specification of this one prevents its occurrence for that one, and the specification of that one prevents its occurrence for this one. Otherwise, this would become that, and that would become this. All of that is impossible, so it is impossible that the power of the servant is like the power of God Almighty. Then it does not follow from that that the power of the servant is such that it is a power of God Almighty — according to the school of the Mu'tazila — because for them a single power between two agents is impossible. Third: The masters of the Mu'tazila: their school is that essences are equal in being essences, and they only differ from each other due to the specialization of each of them with its particular attribute. On this basis: the essence of God Almighty is equal to the rest of the essences in essentiality. Yet it does not follow from that that whatever is valid for each of the essences is also valid for the rest of the essences. Otherwise, it would follow that for the essence of God: temporality, contingency, need for a place, and that for the essences of originated things they are eternal, necessary of existence. All of that is impossible. Fourth: The attribute of existence — according to them — is one attribute. So the existence occurring in black and white is equal to the existence occurring in the essence of God. Yet equality in all judgments does not follow. Fifth: According to them, the accident subsisting in a place prevents its occurrence in a second place, while a similar accident may subsist in the second place. So here the similars in complete quiddity have differed in concomitant properties. Sixth: Two wills attached to one object of will in one aspect are similars. Yet one of them may be in a place and the other not in a place, and the will that occurred in a place is impossible to occur not in a place, and vice versa impossible. Seventh: The accident that does not persist: its coming into existence is confined to a specific time, meaning its coming into existence only at the time it occurred. Then these accidents are similar, just as sounds are similar in complete quiddity. So here equality in complete quiddity is present, yet equality in all judgments does not follow. Eighth: According to them, when the servant performs an act, then it perishes, he is not able to repeat it after non-existence, but may be able to produce its like. So here the similars in complete quiddity are not equal in all judgments. Ninth: Existence is one attribute according to them. Also: according to them, the effect of the agent is nothing but the acquisition of existence. For the quiddities — according to their masters — are established in non-existence, so it is impossible for the agent to have an effect on them. Yet, with this, the servant is able to acquire existence for some quiddities and not for others. Tenth: The substance at the moment of its occurrence is in need of the agent, but during its persistence it is impossible to attribute it to the agent. And the difference between one essence according to two times is like the difference between two different essences. So if it is permissible for one person to differ according to two times, then it is permissible for one person to differ according to two times.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a series of ten counterexamples (with the second omitted) aimed at refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that 'what is valid for a thing is valid for its like'. The author begins by conceding that specification (التعين) is irrelevant, but then argues that the Mu'tazilite principle is invalidated by many instances. The first example shows that individuals of a species are equal in quiddity, yet one cannot become the other because each has a specific identity. Applying this to divine power, the author argues that the servant's power cannot be like God's power, and refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that a single power could be shared between two agents. The third example points out that the Mu'tazilite masters themselves hold that essences are equal in essentiality, yet this does not permit ascribing temporal, contingent, or need-related attributes to God, nor ascribing eternity or necessity to creatures. The fourth example uses the attribute of existence: it is one, but the existence in accidents like black and white is not equal in all respects to God's existence. The fifth example notes that an accident subsisting in one location cannot subsist in another simultaneously, yet its similar can later subsist in that other location, so similars differ in concomitants. The sixth example discusses two wills attached to the same object: one may be in a subject and the other without a subject, and they cannot be exchanged. The seventh example deals with non-persisting accidents that are confined to a specific time, yet sounds are similar in quiddity but not equal in all rulings. The eighth example shows that a servant cannot repeat a perished act but can produce its like, so similars differ. The ninth example discusses existence as one attribute, yet the servant can acquire existence for some quiddities but not others, despite the quiddities being established in non-existence. The tenth example uses the substance: it depends on an agent at origination but not during persistence, so a single essence can differ over time, just as two different essences differ. The author concludes that difference in rulings does not require difference in essence.

Key Terms
التعين
تمام الماهية
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That equality in complete quiddity entails equality in all rulings and attributes.
Correction: The first example shows that even with identical quiddity, individual identity prevents identity of rulings, especially in the case of divine and human power.

Checkpoint
What does the first example demonstrate about individuals of the same type?
A) They are completely identical in all respects
B) They are equal in quiddity but distinct due to specification
C) They become the same if specification is removed
D) They are not equal in quiddity

Key Takeaway
Even if two things share the same quiddity, their individual identities prevent one from being exactly like the other in all rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God's power is incomparable, but do you secretly act as if your own choices are independent of His decree? How does this passage challenge that assumption?
?
When you compare yourself to others, you focus on shared human nature, but do you forget that your unique identity stands before God and cannot be swapped? What does that mean for your personal accountability?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that if two things are equal, they must have the same rulings. Where in your life have you assumed that because others are 'like you' they face the same judgment—ignoring that God's knowledge of each individual is unique?

Third Refutation: Essences and Attributes
Third refutation using Mu'tazilite view on essences and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazilite principle by showing that essences equal in essentiality do not share all judgments, especially regarding God and creation.

Core Idea
Equality in essentiality does not justify ascribing temporal attributes to God or eternal attributes to creatures.

The third example builds on the Mu'tazilite own view that essences (dhawāt) are equal in being essences. If the principle were true, then what applies to created essences would apply to God's essence, leading to absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the third example
Read the Arabic passage about Mu'tazilite view on essences and the implications.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
According to the Mu'tazilite masters, essences are equal in being essences and differ only by specific attributes.
3
See the refutation
If this equality leads to identical judgments, then God's essence would be temporal and contingent, and creatures would be eternal—both impossible.
4
Draw the conclusion
Thus the principle fails: equality in essentiality does not produce equality in all judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What does the third example conclude if the Mu'tazilite principle were applied to essences?
A) God would be temporal and creatures eternal
B) All essences would be identical
C) Attributes would not matter
D) The principle would be confirmed

Key Takeaway
Equality in essentiality does not justify transferring rulings between God and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God as if He is like His creation in qualities you can imagine? In what specific area of your life have you subtly assumed God's attributes are limited by your experience of created attributes?
?
This argument shows that God's essence is entirely unique. Reflect: when you pray, do you truly approach God as utterly unlike anything you know, or do you bring mental images of a being like yourself only greater? What would change if you internalized His absolute uniqueness?
?
The Mu'tazilites' error was applying logic from creation to Creator. Is there any area of your faith where you judge God's actions by human standards? Commit to one concrete change in how you think about God's decree after reading this.

Fourth and Fifth Refutations: Existence and Accidents
Fourth and fifth refutations using existence and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two more refutations: one concerning the attribute of existence as a single attribute, and another concerning accidents and their location-specificity.

Core Idea
A single attribute like existence does not bestow the same judgments on its bearers.

The author now focuses on specific Mu'tazilite doctrines: that existence is one attribute, and that accidents subsisting in one place cannot occur in another simultaneously. These provide further counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth and fifth examples
Read the Arabic and translation for both examples together.
2
Fourth example: existence as one attribute
The Mu'tazilites hold existence as a single attribute; yet existence in accidents differs from existence in God.
3
Fifth example: accidents and location
An accident subsisting in one place cannot simultaneously occur in another, but a similar accident can later subsist in the second place.
4
Draw the common lesson
In both cases, similarity in quiddity does not yield identical rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك التعين شرطاً لحصول ذلك الحكم، في أحد الطرفين، أو مانعاً منه في الطرف الآخر؟. سلمنا: أن التعين ساقط الاعتبار، لكن قولكم: ويصح على الشيء ما يصح على مثله، منقوض بصور كثيرة: الأول: إن أفراد النوع الأخير متساوية في تمام الماهية. مع أن تعين هذا يمنع حصوله لذاك، وتعين ذاك يمتنع حصوله لهذا. وإلا لصار هذا عين ذاك، وذاك عين هذا. وكل ذلك محال فمحال أن يكون مقدور العبد مثل المقدور الله تعالى. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يكون مقدور العبد بحيث أن يكون مقدوراً لله تعالى - على مذهب المعتزلة - لأن عندهم مقدور واحد بين قادرين محال. الثالث: إن مشايخ المعتزلة: مذهبهم: أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وإنما يخالف بعضها بعضاً لأجل اختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته الخاصة. فعلى هذا: ذات الله تعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يصح على كل واحد من الذوات كل ما يصح على سائر الذوات. وإلا لزم أن يصح على ذات الله: الحدوث والإمكان، والحاجة إلى المحل، وأن يصح على ذوات المحدثات كونها قديمة، واجبة الوجود. وكل ذلك محال. الرابع: إن صفة الوجود - عندهم - صفة واحدة. فيكون الوجود الحاصل في السواد والبياض: مساوياً للوجود الحاصل في ذات الله. ولم يلزم الاستواء في جميع الأحكام. الخامس: عندهم العرض القائم بمحل يمنع حلوله في المحل الثاني، مع أن مثل ذلك العرض، قد يكون قائماً بالمحل الثاني. فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، قد اختلفت في اللوازم. السادس: إن الإرادتين المعلقتين بالمراد الواحد، على الوجه الواحد: مثلان. ثم إن إحداهما قد تكون في محل، والأخرى لا في محل، والإرادة التي حصلت في المحل يمتنع حصولها لا في محل وبالعكس يمتنع. السابع: العرض الذي لا يبقى: يختص حدوثه بوقت معين. بمعنى أنه حدوثه إلا في الوقت الذي حدث فيه. ثم إن تلك الأعراض متماثلة كالأصوات متماثلة في تمام الماهية فههنا المساواة في تمام الماهية حاصلة، ولم يلزم منه المساواة في كل الأحكام. الثامن: عندهم العبد إذا فعل فعلا، ثم فني. فإنه لا يقدر على إعادته بعد العدم، وقد يقدر على إيجاد مثله. فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، لم تكن متساوية في كل الأحكام. التاسع: الوجود صفة واحدة عندهم. وأيضاً: فعندهم تأثير القادر ليس إلا في تحصيل الوجود. فإن الماهيات - عند مشايخهم - ثابتة في العدم، فيمتنع أن يكون للقادر فيها تأثير. ثم مع هذا، العبد يقدر على تحصيل الوجود لبعض الماهيات دون البعض. العاشر: الجوهر حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى الفاعل، وحال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل والتفاوت بين الذات الواحدة: بحسب وقتين من التفاوت، بين ذاتين متفاوتين. فإذا جاز أن يختلف في الشخص الواحد بحسب وقتين، فيجوز أن يختلف في الشخص الواحد بحسب وقتين.
That specification is a condition for the occurrence of that judgment in one of the two sides, or a prevention from it in the other side? We concede: that specification is disregarded, but your statement: 'And what is valid for a thing is valid for its like' is refuted by many instances: First: The individuals of the final type are equal in complete quiddity, while the specification of this one prevents its occurrence for that one, and the specification of that one prevents its occurrence for this one. Otherwise, this would become that, and that would become this. All of that is impossible, so it is impossible that the power of the servant is like the power of God Almighty. Then it does not follow from that that the power of the servant is such that it is a power of God Almighty — according to the school of the Mu'tazila — because for them a single power between two agents is impossible. Third: The masters of the Mu'tazila: their school is that essences are equal in being essences, and they only differ from each other due to the specialization of each of them with its particular attribute. On this basis: the essence of God Almighty is equal to the rest of the essences in essentiality. Yet it does not follow from that that whatever is valid for each of the essences is also valid for the rest of the essences. Otherwise, it would follow that for the essence of God: temporality, contingency, need for a place, and that for the essences of originated things they are eternal, necessary of existence. All of that is impossible. Fourth: The attribute of existence — according to them — is one attribute. So the existence occurring in black and white is equal to the existence occurring in the essence of God. Yet equality in all judgments does not follow. Fifth: According to them, the accident subsisting in a place prevents its occurrence in a second place, while a similar accident may subsist in the second place. So here the similars in complete quiddity have differed in concomitant properties. Sixth: Two wills attached to one object of will in one aspect are similars. Yet one of them may be in a place and the other not in a place, and the will that occurred in a place is impossible to occur not in a place, and vice versa impossible. Seventh: The accident that does not persist: its coming into existence is confined to a specific time, meaning its coming into existence only at the time it occurred. Then these accidents are similar, just as sounds are similar in complete quiddity. So here equality in complete quiddity is present, yet equality in all judgments does not follow. Eighth: According to them, when the servant performs an act, then it perishes, he is not able to repeat it after non-existence, but may be able to produce its like. So here the similars in complete quiddity are not equal in all judgments. Ninth: Existence is one attribute according to them. Also: according to them, the effect of the agent is nothing but the acquisition of existence. For the quiddities — according to their masters — are established in non-existence, so it is impossible for the agent to have an effect on them. Yet, with this, the servant is able to acquire existence for some quiddities and not for others. Tenth: The substance at the moment of its occurrence is in need of the agent, but during its persistence it is impossible to attribute it to the agent. And the difference between one essence according to two times is like the difference between two different essences. So if it is permissible for one person to differ according to two times, then it is permissible for one person to differ according to two times.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a series of ten counterexamples (with the second omitted) aimed at refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that 'what is valid for a thing is valid for its like'. The author begins by conceding that specification (التعين) is irrelevant, but then argues that the Mu'tazilite principle is invalidated by many instances. The first example shows that individuals of a species are equal in quiddity, yet one cannot become the other because each has a specific identity. Applying this to divine power, the author argues that the servant's power cannot be like God's power, and refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that a single power could be shared between two agents. The third example points out that the Mu'tazilite masters themselves hold that essences are equal in essentiality, yet this does not permit ascribing temporal, contingent, or need-related attributes to God, nor ascribing eternity or necessity to creatures. The fourth example uses the attribute of existence: it is one, but the existence in accidents like black and white is not equal in all respects to God's existence. The fifth example notes that an accident subsisting in one location cannot subsist in another simultaneously, yet its similar can later subsist in that other location, so similars differ in concomitants. The sixth example discusses two wills attached to the same object: one may be in a subject and the other without a subject, and they cannot be exchanged. The seventh example deals with non-persisting accidents that are confined to a specific time, yet sounds are similar in quiddity but not equal in all rulings. The eighth example shows that a servant cannot repeat a perished act but can produce its like, so similars differ. The ninth example discusses existence as one attribute, yet the servant can acquire existence for some quiddities but not others, despite the quiddities being established in non-existence. The tenth example uses the substance: it depends on an agent at origination but not during persistence, so a single essence can differ over time, just as two different essences differ. The author concludes that difference in rulings does not require difference in essence.

Key Terms
الوجود
العرض
تمام الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the fourth example say about the attribute of existence?
A) It is different in God and in accidents
B) It is one attribute, but equality in all judgments does not follow
C) It is multiple attributes
D) It is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
Sharing a single attribute like existence does not mean sharing all judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because someone shares a label with you (Muslim, human, same sect) they must share all your qualities or judgments? How does this lesson expose the pride or laziness behind that assumption? Instead of judging by surface similarities, where in your life do you need to actively look deeper at what God actually requires from each person uniquely? Will you commit this week to ask God for guidance before categorizing others?
?
Your existence is as contingent as the black and white in the example — utterly dependent on God's will at every moment. When was the last time that truth made you tremble? If you truly knew that your very being is a gift sustained by His power alone, what would change about your prayer, your trust, and your fear of disobeying Him?

Sixth, Seventh, and Eighth Refutations: Wills, Time, and Repetition
Three refutations using wills, time, and repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three more refutations: two wills attached to the same object, accidents confined to a specific time, and the inability to repeat a perished act.

Core Idea
Similarity in quiddity does not guarantee similarity in temporal, locational, or causal properties.

The author continues with examples from psychology, physics, and human action to show that even when things are identical in quiddity, they differ in their rulings due to context.

Guided Path
1
Read the three examples together
Read the Arabic for the sixth, seventh, and eighth examples along with their translations.
2
Sixth example: two wills
Two wills attached to the same object are similar, but one may be in a subject and the other not, and they cannot be exchanged.
3
Seventh example: time-specific accidents
An accident that does not persist is confined to a specific time; similar accidents like sounds occur at different times.
4
Eighth example: inability to repeat
A servant cannot repeat a perished act but can produce a similar one, showing difference in ruling despite similarity in quiddity.
