The Sahaba's Mutual Criticism: A Case Against Certainty in Ahad Reports
Second wajh: hadith scholars transmitted criticism among Sahaba, requiring rejection of reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses examples of criticism among the Sahaba to argue that ahad reports cannot yield certainty in matters of creed.

Core Idea
Sahaba criticized each other's narrations—why trust their isolated reports for creed?

The author presents a powerful historical argument: even the most respected generation, the Sahaba, did not treat each other's isolated narrations as beyond doubt. By examining seven incidents where Companions questioned, rejected, or criticized one another's reports, the author builds a case that ahad reports—even from the Sahaba—cannot provide the certainty required for theological claims. This lesson walks through those examples and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the author lists seven distinct examples. Pay attention to how each example shows suspicion or criticism among the Sahaba.
2
Identify the seven examples and their key features
Go through each of the seven numbered examples. For each, note who criticized whom and what the criticism was (e.g., demanding proof, accusing of falsehood, rejecting a text).
These examples are not random; they escalate from verbal disagreement to physical violence and even rejection of Quranic verses.
3
Understand the underlying argument
The author uses each example to show that the Sahaba themselves did not consider an ahad report from a fellow Companion as certain. They required witnesses, made oaths, or openly rejected narrations. This undermines the reliability of ahad reports for establishing certain knowledge.
The author is not attacking the Sahaba's integrity but highlighting that even they treated ahad reports with caution.
4
Reflect on the implications for creed
Consider the broader point: if ahad reports are not certain, they cannot be used as proof in matters of theology (like attributes of Allah, eschatology, etc.). The author is arguing that only mutawatir reports yield certainty.
This is a foundational argument in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam about the value of ahad vs. mutawatir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : وهو أن المحدثين نقلوا طعن بعض القوم في البعض . وذلك يوجب رد الروايات . بيان المقدمة الأولى من وجوه : أحدها : ما روي أن أبا موسى الأشعري ، استأذن على عمر ، ثلاث مرات . فلم يأذن له ، فرجع فبلغ ذلك عمر . فقال له : ما ردّك ؟ فقال : سمعت النبي - عليه السلام - يقول : ( إذا استأذن أحدكم ثلاث مرات ، فلم يأذن له ، فليرجع ، فقال عمر : لتأتيني ببينة على صحة ما قلت ، وإلا آذيتك وهذا يدل على أن عمر ، كان يتهم أبا موسى في تلك الرواية . وثانيها : المخاصمة الشديدة التي وقعت بين عثمان وبين أبي ذر ، حتى نقل أن عثمان سير أبا ذر إلى الربذة . ونقل أيضاً : أن عثمان ضرب ابن مسعود حتى كسر ضلعان من أضلاعه. وكان كل واحد منهما يعظم الطعن في صاحبه . وأيضاً : إنهم حبسوا عثمان في داره ظلماً وقتلوه . وثالثها : ما نقل أن علياً ، كان يستحلف الرواة . ولولا التهمة ، وإلا لما فعل ذلك . ورابعها : المحادثة التي وقعت بين علي بن أبي طالب ، وبين طلحة والزبير ، وعائشة - رضي الله عنهم - وطعن كل واحد منهم في الآخر . وخامسها : أن ابن مسعود كان يجتث المعوذتين من المصاحف، ويقول : إنهما ليسا من القرآن. وسائر الناس أنكروا عليه ذلك . مع كون القرآن منقولاً على سبيل التواتر . فما ظنك بسائر الأشياء . وسادسها : روي أن معاوية كان يقول على منبر دمشق : إياكم وأحاديث رسول الله - عليه السلام - إلا حديثاً كان في عهد عمر . فإن عمر كان يخيف الناس في الله . ولولا معاوية اتهم أولئك الرواة ، وإلا لم يقل ذلك . وسابعها : ما روى مسلم في صحيحه : أن رجلاً جاء إلى عمر بن الخطاب . فقال : إني أجنبت ، فلم أصب الماء. فقال عمار بن ياسر لعمر بن الخطاب : أما تذكر أنا كنا في سفر أنا وأنت . فأما أنت فلم تصل . وأما أنا
The second: That the hadith scholars transmitted the criticism of some people [i.e., the Companions] against others. And that necessitates the rejection of the narrations. Clarification of the first premise from aspects: One of them: What was narrated that Abu Musa al-Ash'ari sought permission from 'Umar three times, and he did not give him permission, so he returned. And that reached 'Umar. He said to him: What made you return? He said: I heard the Prophet—peace be upon him—say: 'When one of you seeks permission three times and is not given permission, let him return.' 'Umar said: You must bring me evidence for the correctness of what you said, otherwise I will punish you. And this indicates that 'Umar suspected Abu Musa in that narration. The second: The severe quarrel that occurred between 'Uthman and Abu Dharr, to the extent that it was narrated that 'Uthman exiled Abu Dharr to al-Rabadhah. And it was also narrated that 'Uthman beat Ibn Mas'ud until he broke two of his ribs. And each of them greatly criticized his companion. And also: They imprisoned 'Uthman in his house unjustly and killed him. The third: What was narrated that 'Ali used to make the narrators swear oaths. And were it not for suspicion, he would not have done that. The fourth: The conflict that occurred between 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, Talhah, al-Zubayr, and 'A'ishah—may Allah be pleased with them—and each of them criticized the other. The fifth: That Ibn Mas'ud used to remove the mu'awwidhatayn from the copies of the Quran and say: They are not from the Quran. And the rest of the people disapproved of that, despite the Quran being transmitted via tawatur. So what do you think of other things? The sixth: It was narrated that Mu'awiyah used to say on the pulpit of Damascus: Beware of the hadiths of the Messenger of Allah—peace be upon him—except a hadith that was in the time of 'Umar. For 'Umar used to make people fear Allah. And were it not that Mu'awiyah suspected those narrators, he would not have said that. The seventh: What Muslim narrated in his Sahih: That a man came to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and said: I became junub and did not find water. 'Ammar ibn Yasir said to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab: Do you not remember that we were on a journey, I and you? As for you, you did not pray. And as for I...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect (wajh) of the second proof that isolated reports do not yield certainty. The argument is that the hadith scholars themselves have transmitted instances where the Companions criticized one another, which necessitates rejecting their narrations in matters requiring certainty. The author then lists seven examples. First: 'Umar demanded a witness from Abu Musa regarding the hadith of seeking permission three times, showing 'Umar's suspicion. Second: The severe conflict between 'Uthman and Abu Dharr, and 'Uthman's beating of Ibn Mas'ud, leading to mutual criticism. Also the unjust imprisonment and killing of 'Uthman. Third: 'Ali's practice of making narrators swear oaths, indicating suspicion. Fourth: The conflict between 'Ali, Talhah, al-Zubayr, and 'A'ishah, with each criticizing the other. Fifth: Ibn Mas'ud's rejection of the mu'awwidhatayn as part of the Quran, despite the Quran being transmitted by tawatur, implying that if such disagreement can occur in the Quran, then ahad reports are even less reliable. Sixth: Mu'awiyah's warning against most hadiths except from 'Umar's time, implying he suspected narrators. Seventh: The hadith of 'Ammar reminding 'Umar about tayammum, showing 'Umar's forgetfulness or disagreement. The author uses these examples to argue that the Sahaba were not infallible in transmission and even criticized each other, so ahad reports from them cannot be accepted as certain knowledge. The passage ends abruptly, but the list is complete.

Key Terms
طعن (ta'n)
رد الروايات (radd al-riwayat)
الصحابة (al-sahaba)
التواتر (tawatur)
المعوذتين (al-mu'awwidhatayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies the overall reliability of the Sahaba and rejects all hadith.
Correction: The passage does not deny the Sahaba's integrity but uses their own mutual caution to argue that ahad reports from them cannot be considered certain in theological matters. It is a critique of using ahad for creed, not a blanket rejection of all hadith.

Checkpoint
What does the example of 'Umar requiring a witness from Abu Musa indicate according to the author?
A) That 'Umar was a strict ruler
B) That 'Umar suspected Abu Musa in that narration
C) That Abu Musa was not a reliable narrator
D) That the hadith about seeking permission is weak

Key Takeaway
The author uses seven historical incidents of criticism among the Sahaba to argue that ahad reports from them cannot be considered certain, thus they are insufficient for establishing theological truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a hadith that supports a belief you already hold, do you ever pause to check its authenticity and context, or do you accept it because it feels true? This lesson shows that even the Sahaba did not treat each other's reports as certain. How will you change your approach to learning about Allah from hadith?
?
The Sahaba openly criticized one another for the sake of truth. Are you willing to be corrected by others when you might be wrong, especially in matters of faith? Or do you hold onto a position because of pride or attachment?
?
If the Sahaba themselves could disagree and accuse each other, what does that say about your own confidence in isolated opinions from scholars today? Will you now demand stronger evidence before building your creed upon a single report?

The Criticism of 'Ammar's Narration on Tayammum
The author presents and analyzes a criticism against 'Ammar based on his exchange with 'Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the exchange between 'Umar and 'Ammar to construct a kalam-style argument questioning 'Ammar's reliability as a narrator.

Core Idea
A narrator's willingness to suppress a hadith can undermine their credibility.

This passage presents a classic example of how internal evidence from a companion's behavior can be used to critique a hadith narration. The author analyzes the conversation between 'Umar and 'Ammar, drawing a logical conclusion that either way, 'Ammar's reliability is damaged. By examining this reasoning, you'll learn how such critical arguments are structured in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the incident
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the factual basis: 'Ammar's tayammum, the Prophet's ruling, and 'Umar's rebuke.
Note the exact wording of 'Umar's 'Fear Allah' and 'Ammar's response.
2
Identify the two key statements
Focus on 'Umar's 'Fear Allah' (implying suspicion) and 'Ammar's offer to stop narrating (implying willingness to conceal). These are the core pieces of evidence.
This is not explicit accusation, but implicit doubt.
3
Analyze the logical dilemma
The author presents two possibilities: if 'Ammar is truthful, his offer to stop narrating is wrong; if he is lying, his offer confirms the suspicion. Both weaken his reliability.
This is a form of argument by contradiction or necessary consequence.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how this reasoning is used in hadith criticism. It is not about the content of the hadith but about the narrator's behavior.
This shows that external consistency and behavior are considered in evaluating narrators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتيممت فصليت . فذكرت للنبي . فقال عليه السلام : « يكفيك الوجه والكفان ، فقال عمر : اتق الله يا عمار . فقال : إن شئت لم أحدث به . وجه الاستدلال به : أن عمر لما قال لعمار ، اتق الله . دل على أنه اتهمه في هذه الرواية . ولما قال عمار : إن شئت لم أحدث به ، فقد قويت التهمة . لان عماراً إن كان صادقاً في تلك الرواية ، فكيف يجوز له إخفاء الدين ، بسبب كراهية عمر لذلك ، وإن كان كاذباً فقد قويت التهمة
I performed tayammum and prayed. I mentioned it to the Prophet. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The face and the two hands suffice you.' Then 'Umar said: 'Fear Allah, O 'Ammar.' He said: 'If you wish, I will not narrate it.' The aspect of inference from it: When 'Umar said to 'Ammar, 'Fear Allah,' it indicates that he suspected him in this narration. And when 'Ammar said, 'If you wish, I will not narrate it,' the suspicion became stronger. Because if 'Ammar was truthful in that narration, how could it be permissible for him to conceal the religion due to 'Umar's dislike of that? And if he was lying, then the suspicion became stronger.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a kalam-style argument that criticizes the credibility of the Companion 'Ammar ibn Yasir regarding a hadith on tayammum. The author presents the incident: 'Ammar performed tayammum and prayed, then told the Prophet, who confirmed the ruling. Later, 'Umar rebuked 'Ammar saying 'Fear Allah,' implying he doubted the narration. 'Ammar's response—'If you wish, I will not narrate it'—is taken as evidence of weakness in his testimony. The author then analyzes: if 'Ammar was truthful, why would he be willing to suppress a religious ruling because of 'Umar's opinion? That would be concealing the truth. If he was lying, his hesitation confirms the accusation. Thus the argument concludes that the suspicion against 'Ammar is strengthened, regardless of which scenario is true. The reasoning follows the principle of necessary consequence: either way, the narrator's reliability is undermined.

Key Terms
الطعن
التهمة
التيمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage definitively proves that 'Ammar was lying.
Correction: The argument only shows that the narration is questionable based on internal evidence; it does not prove 'Ammar was a liar, but rather that his behavior creates doubt.

Checkpoint
What is the core reasoning used to criticize 'Ammar's reliability?
A) He contradicted the Prophet
B) His willingness to stop narrating if 'Umar wishes implies he might be hiding the truth
C) He was known as a liar
D) 'Umar explicitly called him a liar

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that a narrator's behavior can be used to question their reliability through internal evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a situation where you knew something true but chose to remain silent because of someone's disapproval or authority, just as 'Ammar offered to do? How does this passage expose the danger of letting human fear override the duty to convey truth?
?
When you hear a critique of someone's character or reliability, do you examine both sides of the argument as this passage does, or do you tend to accept the accusation uncritically? What does your response reveal about your own commitment to justice?
?
If you were 'Ammar, standing before 'Umar, what would have been the most courageous response? Why did 'Ammar's offer to suppress the hadith actually weaken his case? What does this teach you about the relationship between truthfulness and willingness to face opposition?

Examples of Criticisms Against Companions
Three instances of criticism against Zaid ibn Arqam and Ibn Abbas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three specific criticisms narrated against Companions and how they are used to question their reliability.

Core Idea
Criticisms of Companions are recorded; they expose internal contradictions.

The author lists three incidents where Companions are criticized by others. These are presented as evidence that even the most prominent Companions were not free from reproach, a tactic used to undermine their authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the three criticisms
Read each criticism carefully in Arabic and English. Note the critic, the target, and the issue.
The criticisms are numbered eighth, ninth, and tenth.
2
Identify the pattern
Notice that each criticism comes from a different context: a sale, a theological claim, and a legal ruling. The author uses them to show variety.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how these criticisms could be used to cast doubt on the reliability of the Companions involved.
Think about the method of jarh wa ta'dil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثامنها : نقل أن عائشة قالت : اخبروا زيد بن أرقم ، أنه أحبط جهاده مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بسبب مسألة العينة . وتاسعها : أن ابن عباس ، لما قال : إن رسول الله عليه السلام ، رأى محمداً ربه ليلة المعراج . قالت عائشة : وقف شعري مما سمعت . من قال : إن رأى ربه ، فقد أعظم الفرية على الله . وهذا الكلام منها : طعن عظيم في ابن عباس . وعاشرها : ما روى أن ابن عباس لم يقبل خبر أبي سعيد في ربا الفضل وتمسك بما رواه عن أسامة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ( لا ربا إلا في النسيئة ) . وفي الجملة : فهذا القدر القليل يرشدك إلى وجدان أمثال هذه المطاعن في كتب التواريخ . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : هذا يوجب تطرق الطعن إليهم . لأن الطاعن ، إن صدق فقد صار المطعون فيه مجروحاً ، وإن كذب فقد صار الطاعن مجروحاً . وكيف كان فالجرح لازم
The eighth: It is reported that 'Aisha said: 'Inform Zaid ibn Arqam that his jihad with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is nullified because of the issue of 'aynah (a type of sale).' The ninth: That when Ibn 'Abbas said that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saw his Lord on the night of Mi'raj, 'Aisha said: 'My hair stood on end from what I heard. Whoever says he saw his Lord has invented a great lie against Allah.' This speech of hers is a great criticism of Ibn 'Abbas. The tenth: That it is reported that Ibn 'Abbas did not accept the report of Abu Sa'id concerning riba al-fadl and adhered to what he narrated from Usama that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no riba except in credit transactions.' In summary: This small amount guides you to find similar criticisms in history books. And if this is established, we say: This necessitates criticism touching them. Because the critic, if truthful, then the criticized person becomes discredited; and if lying, then the critic becomes discredited. In any case, discreditation is necessary.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct instances of criticism against Companions. The first involves 'Aisha declaring that Zaid ibn Arqam's reward for jihad is nullified due to a transaction called 'aynah (a type of sale). The second is 'Aisha's strong rejection of Ibn 'Abbas's claim that the Prophet saw Allah on the night of Mi'raj, calling it a great lie. The third is Ibn 'Abbas preferring the hadith of Usama over that of Abu Sa'id regarding riba al-fadl, implying that Ibn 'Abbas did not consider Abu Sa'id's narration reliable. The author then provides a general principle: when a criticism (ta'n) is made against a Companion, it necessarily discredits someone—either the critic (if false) or the criticized (if true). Thus, discreditation (jarh) is inevitable in such cases. This shows the author's method of exposing internal contradictions and the necessary consequences of such criticisms.

Key Terms
الطعن
العينة
ربا الفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These criticisms are accepted as historical facts that discredit the Companions.
Correction: The author presents them as examples of criticisms, not necessarily as valid. They are used to show the existence of such claims.

Checkpoint

The Principle of Jarh: Inevitable Discreditation
General rule about jarh following from examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that any criticism (ta'n) necessarily leads to discreditation (jarh) of either the critic or the criticized.

Core Idea
Every criticism inevitably discredits someone; jarh is unavoidable.

After presenting examples of criticisms, the author now derives a principle: whenever a criticism is made, someone is discredited. This argument seeks to show that the existence of such criticisms itself creates a problem for the reliability of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'وفي الجملة' and its translation. Understand the logical structure.
2
Analyze the logic
The author presents a dichotomy: if the critic is truthful, then the criticized is discredited; if the critic is lying, then the critic is discredited. Either way, someone is discredited.
This is a classic argument in hadith criticism.
3
Consider the consequence
Why does the author say 'jarh is necessary'? Because the existence of any criticism forces us to apply discreditation to someone.
Think about how this undermines the reliability of the Companions as a whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثامنها : نقل أن عائشة قالت : اخبروا زيد بن أرقم ، أنه أحبط جهاده مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بسبب مسألة العينة . وتاسعها : أن ابن عباس ، لما قال : إن رسول الله عليه السلام ، رأى محمداً ربه ليلة المعراج . قالت عائشة : وقف شعري مما سمعت . من قال : إن رأى ربه ، فقد أعظم الفرية على الله . وهذا الكلام منها : طعن عظيم في ابن عباس . وعاشرها : ما روى أن ابن عباس لم يقبل خبر أبي سعيد في ربا الفضل وتمسك بما رواه عن أسامة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ( لا ربا إلا في النسيئة ) . وفي الجملة : فهذا القدر القليل يرشدك إلى وجدان أمثال هذه المطاعن في كتب التواريخ . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : هذا يوجب تطرق الطعن إليهم . لأن الطاعن ، إن صدق فقد صار المطعون فيه مجروحاً ، وإن كذب فقد صار الطاعن مجروحاً . وكيف كان فالجرح لازم
The eighth: It is reported that 'Aisha said: 'Inform Zaid ibn Arqam that his jihad with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is nullified because of the issue of 'aynah (a type of sale).' The ninth: That when Ibn 'Abbas said that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saw his Lord on the night of Mi'raj, 'Aisha said: 'My hair stood on end from what I heard. Whoever says he saw his Lord has invented a great lie against Allah.' This speech of hers is a great criticism of Ibn 'Abbas. Because the critic, if truthful, then the criticized person becomes discredited; and if lying, then the critic becomes discredited. In any case, discreditation is necessary.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct instances of criticism against Companions. The first involves 'Aisha declaring that Zaid ibn Arqam's reward for jihad is nullified due to a transaction called 'aynah (a type of sale). The second is 'Aisha's strong rejection of Ibn 'Abbas's claim that the Prophet saw Allah on the night of Mi'raj, calling it a great lie. The third is Ibn 'Abbas preferring the hadith of Usama over that of Abu Sa'id regarding riba al-fadl, implying that Ibn 'Abbas did not consider Abu Sa'id's narration reliable. The author then provides a general principle: when a criticism (ta'n) is made against a Companion, it necessarily discredits someone—either the critic (if false) or the criticized (if true). Thus, discreditation (jarh) is inevitable in such cases. This shows the author's method of exposing internal contradictions and the necessary consequences of such criticisms.

Key Terms
الجرح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of criticisms proves that the Companions are unreliable.
Correction: The author is not proving that; he is pointing out a logical consequence that defenders of the Companions must address.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the necessary consequence of any criticism (ta'n)?
A) Only the critic is discredited
B) Only the criticized is discredited
C) Someone is always discredited
D) No one is discredited

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the mere existence of a criticism forces discreditation upon someone, making jarh unavoidable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak critically of someone, do you truly believe your words are truthful? If they are not, what does that make you according to this passage? Will you examine your intentions before speaking next time?
?
Think of a time you were criticized. Did you immediately dismiss the criticism without considering its effect on the critic? Can you now see how that criticism, whether true or false, discredited someone? How does that change your reaction?
?
The author states that jarh is inevitable from any criticism. In your daily conversations, how often do you make criticisms that could discredit someone—perhaps even yourself? What concrete step will you take today to avoid unnecessary jarh?

First criticism: Fasting while junub hadith
First criticism: Abu Hurairah's hadith on fasting while junub is contradicted, and his informant is shown dead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first criticism against Abu Hurairah's narration about fasting while in a state of janabah, focusing on the chain issue.

Core Idea
A hadith's chain can be broken if the narrator's source is proven dead.

The first criticism against Abu Hurairah involves a hadith about fasting while junub. When other Companions checked with 'Aisha and Umm Salamah, they contradicted it. Abu Hurairah then admitted his source was dead at the time. This lesson examines that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the claim and counterclaim.
The passage is part of a larger criticism series.
2
Identify the contradiction
Note how 'Aisha and Umm Salamah's statement contradicts Abu Hurairah's hadith.
3
Understand the chain problem
Abu Hurairah claimed Al-Fadl ibn 'Abbas as his source, but Al-Fadl was dead, breaking the chain.
This is the core of the criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في تقرير هذا الطعن : إن كثيراً من الصحابة طعنوا في أبي هريرة . وبيانه من وجوه : أحدها : إن أبا هريرة روى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ( من أصبح جنباً فلا صوم له ، فرجعوا في هذه المسألة إلى عائشة ، وأم سلمة . فقالتا : كان النبي عليه السلام يصبح جنباً ، ثم يصوم . فذكروا ذلك لأبي هريرة . فقال : هما أعلم بذلك . أنبأني بهذا الخبر : الفضل بن عباس . واتفق أنه كان ميتاً في ذلك الوقت . وثانيها : أنه لما روى قوله عليه السلام : « إذا استيقظ أحدكم فلا يغمسن يده في الإناء ، حتى يغسلها ثلاثاً ، قال ابن عباس كالمنكر لقوله : فما تصنع بمهر أستا ؟
The third aspect in establishing this criticism: That many Companions criticized Abu Hurairah. And its explanation includes several points: First: That Abu Hurairah narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever wakes up in a state of janabah, there is no fasting for him.' So they referred this issue to 'Aisha and Umm Salamah. They said: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) would wake up in a state of janabah and then fast.' They mentioned this to Abu Hurairah. He said: 'They know better about that. Al-Fadl ibn 'Abbas informed me of this report.' And it happened that he was dead at that time. Second: That when he narrated his saying (peace be upon him): 'When one of you wakes up, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times,' Ibn 'Abbas said, as if rejecting his statement: 'Then what do you do with the dowry of a wife [?]?'
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage begins the third set of criticisms, focusing on Abu Hurairah. The author states that many Companions criticized him, and then gives six proofs (wujuh). The first proof: Abu Hurairah narrated a hadith that a person in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah) in the morning cannot fast. When others asked 'Aisha and Umm Salamah, they contradicted him, saying the Prophet would wake up in janabah and then fast. When confronted, Abu Hurairah acknowledged their superior knowledge and claimed he received the report from Al-Fadl ibn 'Abbas, but it turned out that Al-Fadl was dead at that time—implying the chain is broken or the report is unreliable. The second proof: Abu Hurairah narrated a hadith about not dipping the hand into a vessel before washing it upon waking. Ibn 'Abbas objected with a rhetorical question: 'Then what do you do with the dowry of a wife?'—implying that the ruling is impractical or unreasonable, thus casting doubt on the hadith. Both examples show that Abu Hurairah's narrations were challenged by other Companions on grounds of contradiction and impracticality.

Key Terms
الطعن
أبو هريرة
الجنابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The criticism is about the content of the hadith being false.
Correction: The criticism is actually about the chain: Abu Hurairah's source was dead, so the hadith cannot be accepted as authentic.

Checkpoint
What was the main problem with Abu Hurairah's report about fasting while junub?
A) It contradicted the Quran
B) Abu Hurairah's claimed source was dead
C) 'Aisha and Umm Salamah were not reliable
D) The report was about tayammum

Key Takeaway
The first criticism highlights that a narrator's claim of a source can be invalidated if that source is proven to be dead at the time of narration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief confidently, and then encounter strong evidence that contradicts it, do you immediately defend your position or do you pause to consider you might be wrong? Can you recall a time when you had to admit being mistaken about a religious matter?
?
Abu Hurairah deferred to Aisha and Umm Salamah saying 'they know better.' Is your heart humble enough to accept correction from those with more knowledge, even if it challenges what you have long believed? What would it take for you to change a deeply held opinion?
?
Are there 'dead sources' in your faith—teachings or practices you follow without verifying their chain of authenticity? How can you ensure that what you believe and practice is grounded in reliable, authentic evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah?

Second criticism: Ibn 'Abbas objects to Abu Hurairah's hand-washing hadith with a rhetorical question.

A Companion's objection can expose a hadith's impracticality or weakness.

The second criticism involves a hadith about not dipping hands into a vessel before washing them upon waking. Ibn 'Abbas responded with a sarcastic question, implying the ruling is absurd. This lesson examines that exchange.

Ibn 'Abbas questions the practicality of the ruling with a rhetorical question.
3

The objection implies the hadith is problematic, likely due to chain or content issues.

Umar's Prohibition of Abu Hurairah
Analysis of Umar's prohibition as a criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma the author presents from Umar's action against Abu Hurairah.

Core Idea
Umar's beating creates a dilemma that discredits someone.

In this passage, the author presents the third criticism against Abu Hurairah: the report that Umar ibn al-Khattab physically prevented him from narrating hadith. The author analyzes this incident using a powerful either-or logic. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the presentation of the incident and the logical analysis.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author presents two conditional statements: if Abu Hurairah was truthful, then Umar is criticized; if Abu Hurairah was suspect, then he himself is criticized.
This is a classic dilemma.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that in either case, someone is discredited. This is the force of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : ما روي أن عمر رضي الله عنه منع أبا هريرة عن الرواية ، وعلاه بالدرة . فنقول : أبو هريرة إن كان صادقاً في تلك الروايات ، صار عمر مطعوناً فيه ، بسبب ذلك المنع ، وإن كان متهماً فقد صار أبو هريرة مطعوناً . ورابعها : إن أبا هريرة ، كان يقول : ( حدثني خليلي أبو القاسم ، فمنعه علي بن أبي طالب . وقال : متى كان خليلا لك ؟
Third: That it was reported that 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating and beat him with a whip. So we say: Abu Hurairah, if he was truthful in those narrations, then 'Umar becomes criticized because of that prevention; and if he was suspect, then Abu Hurairah becomes criticized. Fourth: That Abu Hurairah used to say: 'My intimate friend (khalili) Abul-Qasim told me,' so 'Ali ibn Abi Talib prevented him and said: 'When was he an intimate friend to you?'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth proofs in the criticism of Abu Hurairah. The third: It is reported that 'Umar physically prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating hadith and beat him. The author then analyzes this incident using the same disjunctive logic as before: either Abu Hurairah was truthful, in which case 'Umar's action would make him (Umar) subject to criticism for suppressing truth; or Abu Hurairah was unreliable, in which case he himself is criticized. Either way, someone is discredited. The fourth: Abu Hurairah used to say 'My intimate friend (khalili) Abul-Qasim (a name of the Prophet) told me,' but 'Ali objected, questioning when Abu Hurairah had become such a close friend of the Prophet. 'Ali's objection implies that Abu Hurairah was exaggerating his relationship with the Prophet, thus casting doubt on his narrations. Both incidents show that senior Companions like 'Umar and 'Ali openly challenged Abu Hurairah's reliability.

Key Terms
المنع
الدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is accusing Umar of wrongdoing.
Correction: The author uses this incident to show that either Umar or Abu Hurairah must be criticized, not to assert Umar's fault.

Checkpoint
What logical outcome does the author derive from Umar's prohibition of Abu Hurairah?
A) Umar was wrong to beat Abu Hurairah.
B) Abu Hurairah was definitely a liar.
C) Either Umar becomes criticized if Abu Hurairah was truthful, or Abu Hurairah becomes criticized if he was suspect.
D) The hadith should be accepted regardless.

Key Takeaway
The incident of Umar beating Abu Hurairah creates a dilemma that inevitably discredits one of the two Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a beloved figure like Umar or Abu Hurairah, do you immediately reject it or examine the evidence? Are you willing to accept that even the best of people can be subject to criticism without diminishing your love for them? Reflect on a specific instance where you defended someone without looking at both sides.
?
This dilemma forces you to choose between two uncomfortable possibilities. If you knew that a disagreement between two righteous individuals meant one of them must be in error, how would that affect your view of the Companions? Do you hold them as infallible or human? Confront the discomfort of accepting that even the greatest can be wrong in some matters.
?
The author uses a logical dilemma to make his point. Are you able to apply similar critical thinking when evaluating religious arguments, or do you rely on emotions and loyalties? Challenge yourself to examine one belief you hold strongly and see if it withstands a logical dilemma like this one.

Presentation of Ali's objection as a criticism.

The fourth criticism is based on an incident where Ali ibn Abi Talib directly challenged Abu Hurairah's use of the term 'khalili' (intimate friend) to describe his relationship with the Prophet. This passage shows how a senior Companion questioned Abu Hurairah's credibility.

Al-Bara's Accusation Against Abu Hurairah
Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib accuses Abu Hurairah of narrating unverified reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth criticism against Abu Hurairah: that he narrated what he did not hear, as claimed by Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib.

Core Idea
Al-Bara' directly accused Abu Hurairah of narrating what he never heard.

The author presents the fifth argument against Abu Hurairah, citing a Companion's direct accusation. Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib claimed that he and others heard the same as Abu Hurairah, but Abu Hurairah narrated things they never heard. This challenges his reliability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the directness of the accusation.
2
Identify the accuser
Who is making the accusation? Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib was a Companion. Consider his credibility.
3
Understand the accusation
What exactly is he saying? 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' This implies fabrication.
The 'they' refers to Abu Hurairah and perhaps others.
4
Reflect on the implication
If the accusation is accepted, it casts doubt on Abu Hurairah's entire corpus.
But note that this is part of a polemic; many scholars defended Abu Hurairah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : إن البراء بن عازب، طعن في أبي هريرة . وقال : سمعنا كما سمعوا . لكنهم حدثوا بما لم يسمعوا . وسادسها : إن الأفاضل الذين صحبوا من أول المبعث إلى آخر الوفاة ما حدثوا إلا قليلا . كأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهم - مع كثرة علومهم وقوة خواطرهم ، وشدة ملازمتهم لحضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وجد النبي في إرشادهم وتعليمهم . وأما أبو هريرة فإنه لم يصل الى خدمة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا مدة قليلة ، وهو في نفسه ، ما كان زائداً في الذكاء والفطنة على أبي بكر وعلي ثم إنه روى ألفي خبر ، وأكثر . وهو نصف الصحاح . وهذا يدل على الطعن الشديد . لأنه لو قدر في تلك المدة القليلة على تحصيل هذه العلوم الكثيرة ، مع أن أبا بكر وعلياً ، ما قدرا على عشر تحصيل تلك العلوم ، في المدة الطويلة ، لوجب القطع بأن أبا هريرة ، كان أفضل منهما ، وأكثر علماً منها . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل
Fifth: That Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib criticized Abu Hurairah and said: 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' Sixth: That the eminent Companions who accompanied [the Prophet] from the beginning of the mission to the end of his life narrated only a few [hadiths], such as Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with them), despite their abundance of knowledge, strength of intellect, and intense attachment to the presence of the Prophet— and [؟] the Prophet in guiding and teaching them. As for Abu Hurairah, he only came to serve the Prophet for a short period, and he himself was not superior in intelligence and acumen to Abu Bakr and 'Ali. Then he narrated two thousand reports or more, which is half of the Sahih [collections]. This indicates severe criticism. Because if it were possible for him to acquire all this abundant knowledge in that short period, while Abu Bakr and 'Ali could not acquire even a tenth of that knowledge over a long period, then it would necessarily follow that Abu Hurairah was better than them and more knowledgeable. And it is known that this is false.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage concludes the list of criticisms against Abu Hurairah with two final points. The fifth proof: Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib directly accused Abu Hurairah and other narrators of transmitting what they had not actually heard from the Prophet, implying they fabricated or exaggerated. The sixth and longest proof argues from the principle of parity: the senior Companions (Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali) spent far longer with the Prophet, were more intelligent and learned, yet narrated very few hadiths. In contrast, Abu Hurairah accompanied the Prophet only briefly, was not exceptional in intelligence, yet narrated thousands of reports (half of the Sahih). The author argues that if it were possible for Abu Hurairah to acquire such vast knowledge in a short time, he would necessarily be superior to Abu Bakr and 'Ali, which is absurd. Therefore, the abundance of his narrations is evidence against his reliability. This is a classic argument from common sense: disproportionate output relative to input indicates unreliability.

Key Terms
الطعن
البراء بن عازب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Bara's accusation is universally accepted as true.
Correction: Al-Bara's statement is itself contested; other scholars have defended Abu Hurairah's integrity.

Checkpoint
What did Al-Bara' say about Abu Hurairah?
A) He was a liar.
B) He narrated what he did not hear.
C) He was ignorant.
D) He never met the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib directly accused Abu Hurairah of narrating hadiths he had not actually heard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about someone without being absolutely certain of the truth, even if you thought it was harmless? How does Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib's accusation—based on what he claimed they 'did not hear'—challenge you to examine your own speech and its accuracy?
?
If someone said about you 'they narrate what they did not hear,' how would that affect your reputation and relationships? Now consider: are you equally careful not to attribute words to others without verification, especially in matters of religion?

The Argument from Disproportionate Narration
The author compares Abu Hurairah's hadith output with that of the senior Companions to argue against his reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth criticism against Abu Hurairah: that his vast number of narrations is disproportionate to his short companionship with the Prophet, especially compared to senior Companions.

Core Idea
If Abu Hurairah were reliable, he would be superior to the senior Companions, which is untenable.

The author presents a more extended argument: the senior Companions (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) spent decades with the Prophet, were more intelligent, yet narrated few hadiths. Abu Hurairah, with short companionship, narrated thousands. This disparity, the author argues, invalidates his reliability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the comparison being made.
2
Identify the two groups compared
Group 1: Senior Companions (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali). Group 2: Abu Hurairah.
Note the attributes of each group.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument is: if short companionship yields many hadiths, then Abu Hurairah would be more knowledgeable than the seniors, which is false. Therefore, the many hadiths are unreliable.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Recognize the underlying assumption
The assumption is that the number of hadiths should roughly correspond to the length of companionship and intellectual capacity.
Defenders of Abu Hurairah argue he had exceptional memory and dedicated himself to memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : إن البراء بن عازب، طعن في أبي هريرة . وقال : سمعنا كما سمعوا . لكنهم حدثوا بما لم يسمعوا . وسادسها : إن الأفاضل الذين صحبوا من أول المبعث إلى آخر الوفاة ما حدثوا إلا قليلا . كأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهم - مع كثرة علومهم وقوة خواطرهم ، وشدة ملازمتهم لحضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وجد النبي في إرشادهم وتعليمهم . وأما أبو هريرة فإنه لم يصل الى خدمة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا مدة قليلة ، وهو في نفسه ، ما كان زائداً في الذكاء والفطنة على أبي بكر وعلي ثم إنه روى ألفي خبر ، وأكثر . وهو نصف الصحاح . وهذا يدل على الطعن الشديد . لأنه لو قدر في تلك المدة القليلة على تحصيل هذه العلوم الكثيرة ، مع أن أبا بكر وعلياً ، ما قدرا على عشر تحصيل تلك العلوم ، في المدة الطويلة ، لوجب القطع بأن أبا هريرة ، كان أفضل منهما ، وأكثر علماً منها . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل
Fifth: That Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib criticized Abu Hurairah and said: 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' Sixth: That the eminent Companions who accompanied [the Prophet] from the beginning of the mission to the end of his life narrated only a few [hadiths], such as Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with them), despite their abundance of knowledge, strength of intellect, and intense attachment to the presence of the Prophet— and [؟] the Prophet in guiding and teaching them. As for Abu Hurairah, he only came to serve the Prophet for a short period, and he himself was not superior in intelligence and acumen to Abu Bakr and 'Ali. Then he narrated two thousand reports or more, which is half of the Sahih [collections]. This indicates severe criticism. Because if it were possible for him to acquire all this abundant knowledge in that short period, while Abu Bakr and 'Ali could not acquire even a tenth of that knowledge over a long period, then it would necessarily follow that Abu Hurairah was better than them and more knowledgeable. And it is known that this is false.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage concludes the list of criticisms against Abu Hurairah with two final points. The fifth proof: Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib directly accused Abu Hurairah and other narrators of transmitting what they had not actually heard from the Prophet, implying they fabricated or exaggerated. The sixth and longest proof argues from the principle of parity: the senior Companions (Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali) spent far longer with the Prophet, were more intelligent and learned, yet narrated very few hadiths. In contrast, Abu Hurairah accompanied the Prophet only briefly, was not exceptional in intelligence, yet narrated thousands of reports (half of the Sahih). The author argues that if it were possible for Abu Hurairah to acquire such vast knowledge in a short time, he would necessarily be superior to Abu Bakr and 'Ali, which is absurd. Therefore, the abundance of his narrations is evidence against his reliability. This is a classic argument from common sense: disproportionate output relative to input indicates unreliability.

Key Terms
الصحاح
الأفاضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the number of hadiths alone indicates reliability.
Correction: The argument here is that the sheer number relative to context suggests fabrication; but defenders argue that Abu Hurairah had a good memory and devoted himself to memorization.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth criticism, why is Abu Hurairah's large number of hadiths suspicious?
A) He was known to lie.
B) Because senior Companions with longer companionship and greater intelligence narrated fewer.
C) He never traveled.
D) The Prophet forbade him.

Key Takeaway
The argument claims that Abu Hurairah's abundant narrations are implausible given his short time with the Prophet and compared to senior Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged the reliability of someone's testimony based on how much they spoke rather than the content? The argument here uses quantity as a weapon. Do you have a tendency to trust those who speak little and dismiss those who speak much? What would it take for you to listen without prejudice?
?
The author argues that if Abu Hurairah's vast knowledge came in a short time, he would surpass the senior Companions—an absurdity. Think of a person in your life whom you underestimate because their background seems limited. Could your assumption be a form of arrogance? Will you ask Allah to protect you from dismissing His gifts to others?
?
This lesson forces you to confront a logical principle: outcomes must be proportionate to inputs. Apply this to your own spiritual output. Does your worship, knowledge, or service reflect the decades of opportunity you have had? If not, what are you doing with the time Allah has given you?

First Two Objections: Chain and Ibn Abbas
Author presents first two objections: a dead narrator and Ibn Abbas's denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two objections raised against Abu Hurairah's hadith narrations: the issue of a dead narrator and Ibn Abbas's questioning of a hadith.

Core Idea
Two early objections challenge Abu Hurairah’s reliability through a broken chain and a companion’s doubt.

The author begins his critique of Abu Hurairah by presenting specific incidents that cast doubt on his narrations. The first involves a report from al-Fadl ibn Abbas—who was dead at the time—indicating a chain problem. The second shows Ibn Abbas directly questioning a hadith about washing hands after waking. These objections set the stage for a broader attack on Abu Hurairah's reliability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the two objections. Notice how the first objection is a brief statement about al-Fadl ibn Abbas's death, and the second is a dialogue.
The phrase 'فما تصنع بمهر أستا' is a rhetorical question.
2
Analyze the first objection
Consider why reporting from a person who was dead at the time of the report would be problematic. This is a clear chain issue.
This is a fundamental critique in hadith criticism.
3
Analyze the second objection
Ibn Abbas's question seems to challenge the practicality of the hadith. Ask yourself: Does his question imply rejection of the hadith or just confusion?
Notice that Ibn Abbas is a well-known authority.

أعلم بذلك . أنبأني بهذا الخبر : الفضل بن عباس . واتفق أنه كان ميتاً في ذلك الوقت . وثانيها : أنه لما روى قوله عليه السلام : « إذا استيقظ أحدكم فلا يغمسن يده في الإناء ، حتى يغسلها ثلاثاً ، قال ابن عباس كالمنكر لقوله : فما تصنع بمهر أستا ؟ وثالثها : ما روي أن عمر رضي الله عنه منع أبا هريرة عن الرواية ، وعلاه بالدرة . فنقول : أبو هريرة إن كان صادقاً في تلك الروايات ، صار عمر مطعوناً فيه ، بسبب ذلك المنع ، وإن كان متهماً فقد صار أبو هريرة مطعوناً ورابعها : إن أبا هريرة ، كان يقول : ( حدثني خليلي أبو القاسم ، فمنعه علي بن أبي طالب . وقال : متى كان خليلا لك ؟ . وخامسها : إن البراء بن عازب، طعن في أبي هريرة . وقال : سمعنا كما سمعوا . لكنهم حدثوا بما لم يسمعوا . وسادسها : إن الأفاضل الذين صحبوا من أول المبعث إلى آخر الوفاة ما حدثوا إلا قليلا . كأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهم - مع كثرة علومهم وقوة خواطرهم ، وشدة ملازمتهم لحضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وجـد النبي في إرشادهم وتعليمهم . وأما أبو هريرة فإنه لم يصل الى خدمة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا مـدة قليلة ، وهو في نفسه ، ما كان زائداً في الذكاء والفطنة على أبي بكر وعلي ثم إنه روى ألفي خبر ، وأكثر . وهو نصف الصحاح . وهذا يدل على الطعن الشديد . لأنه لو قدر في تلك المدة القليلة على تحصيل هذه العلوم الكثيرة ، مع أن أبا بكر وعلياً ، ما قدرا على عشر تحصيل تلك العلوم ، في المدة الطويلة ، لوجب القطع بأن أبا هريرة ، كان أفضل منهما ، وأكثر علماً منها . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ولا يقال : إن سائر الصحابة كانوا مشغولين بالتجارات ، وطلب الدنيا وأن أبا هريرة كان ملازماً للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم . لانا نقول : إن أبا بكر ترك التجارة بالكلية ، واشتغل بخدمة النبي . وفي زمان الإمامة ، كان مقدم الأمة ، والمرجوع إليه في حل المشكلات ، وإزاحة المعضلات . فكان هو أولى بالرواية . فحيث لم يفعل ذلك ، علمنا أن تركه هذه الروايات كان أولى . باتفاق أكابر الصحابة . وحينئذ يتوجه الطعن في أبي هريرة .
I know that. He informed me of this report: al-Fadl ibn Abbas. And it happened that he was dead at that time. The second [objection]: that when he narrated his saying, peace be upon him: 'When one of you wakes up, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times,' Ibn Abbas said, as if denying his saying: 'Then what do you do with the Mahr?' The third: what was narrated that Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating and struck him with a whip. We say: If Abu Hurairah was truthful in those narrations, then Umar becomes criticized because of that prevention, and if he was accused, then Abu Hurairah becomes criticized. The fourth: that Abu Hurairah used to say: 'My friend Abu al-Qasim told me,' so Ali ibn Abi Talib prevented him and said: 'When was he [your] friend?' The fifth: that al-Bara' ibn 'Azib criticized Abu Hurairah and said: 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' The sixth: that the virtuous ones who accompanied [the Prophet] from the beginning of the mission until the end of his death narrated only a little, like Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali - may Allah be pleased with them - with their abundance of knowledge, strength of minds, and intense adherence to the presence of the Prophet, and the Prophet's effort in guiding and teaching them. As for Abu Hurairah, he only reached the service of the Prophet for a short period, and in himself he was not superior in intelligence and acumen to Abu Bakr and Ali, then he narrated two thousand reports, and more, which is half of the Sahihs. This indicates severe criticism. Because if he was able in that short period to acquire this abundant knowledge, while Abu Bakr and Ali were not able to acquire a tenth of that knowledge in the long period, then it would be necessary to decide that Abu Hurairah was better than them and more knowledgeable than them. And it is known that that is false. And it should not be said that the rest of the Companions were busy with trades and seeking the world, and that Abu Hurairah was adherent to the Prophet. For we say: Abu Bakr left trade completely and engaged in the service of the Prophet. And during the time of leadership, he was the foremost of the nation and the one referred to in solving problems and removing difficulties. So he was more entitled to narration. Since he did not do that, we know that his leaving these narrations was better by agreement of the senior Companions. Therefore, the criticism against Abu Hurairah is directed.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections (طعون) against Abu Hurairah's narrations. The author begins with a report from al-Fadl ibn Abbas that seems to conclude a previous point. Then the second objection: Ibn Abbas appears to question a hadith about not dipping a hand after waking by asking a rhetorical question. The third: Umar prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating and struck him; the author argues either Abu Hurairah is truthful, making Umar at fault, or he is suspect, making himself at fault. The fourth: Ali ibn Abi Talib objected to Abu Hurairah calling the Prophet his friend. The fifth: al-Bara' ibn 'Azib claimed that others narrated what they didn't hear. The sixth, most detailed: the senior companions narrated little despite long companionship and intelligence, while Abu Hurairah, with short service and no superiority, narrated two thousand hadith (half of the Sahihs). This implies either Abu Hurairah is superior (false) or his narrations are unreliable. The author then refutes the excuse that others were busy with trade by noting that Abu Bakr left trade and was the foremost in knowledge and leadership, so he should have narrated more; his refraining from narrating indicates it was better, by consensus of senior companions, thus the criticism against Abu Hurairah is valid.

Key Terms
طعن
أبو هريرة
رواية
ابن عباس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Abbas's question was just a simple inquiry, not a rejection.
Correction: The author explicitly says 'as if denying his saying' (كالمنكر لقوله), indicating that Ibn Abbas was expressing doubt or denial.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection about?
A) Abu Hurairah's memory
B) A dead narrator
C) Umar's opposition
D) Ali's comment

Key Takeaway
The first two objections highlight chain issues and companion questioning, undermining Abu Hurairah's reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a hadith you hold dear, does it unsettle you or push you to investigate? How can you respond in a way that strengthens your faith rather than weakens it?
?
Ibn Abbas, a respected Companion, questioned a hadith. Are you quick to dismiss objections from others, or do you consider them with humility? What would it take for you to change your view on a religious matter?
?
The first objection involves a chain with a dead narrator. How often do you verify the sources of your knowledge? Are you content with hearsay, or do you strive to trace what you learn back to reliable origins?

Objections from Umar and Ali
Author presents objections from Umar and Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth objections: Umar's prevention of Abu Hurairah's narration and Ali's objection to his use of 'my friend' for the Prophet.

Core Idea
Umar’s striking and Ali’s denial show senior companions’ skepticism toward Abu Hurairah.

The author continues his list of objections with two incidents involving the most senior companions. Umar is reported to have prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating and even struck him, raising questions about his credibility. Ali similarly objected when Abu Hurairah called the Prophet his 'friend.' These incidents from leading figures cast serious doubt on Abu Hurairah's narrations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third and fourth objections. Note the different nature of each: Umar's action is physical, Ali's is verbal.
2
Analyze Umar's objection
The author presents a dilemma: if Abu Hurairah was truthful, Umar's prevention makes Umar suspect; if Abu Hurairah was untruthful, then he himself is suspect. Either way, it's a problem.
This is a clever argument that forces a choice.
3
Analyze Ali's objection
Ali asks 'When was he your friend?' implying that Abu Hurairah's claim of close companionship with the Prophet is exaggerated.
The term 'خليل' implies a special friendship.

?

?

Al-Bara's Accusation and the Quantity Argument
Author presents al-Bara' s objection and a quantitative argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final two objections: al-Bara' ibn 'Azib's accusation that others narrated what they didn't hear, and the argument from the quantity of narrations compared to senior companions.

Core Idea
A companion's accusation and the vast number of narrations from a short companion’s tenure fuel the criticism.

The last two objections are the most detailed. First, al-Bara' ibn 'Azib charges that some companions narrated what they did not actually hear. Then the author presents a lengthy argument: despite the senior companions like Abu Bakr and Ali having longer companionship and greater knowledge, Abu Hurairah narrated half of all sound hadith in a short period—an implausible claim that points to unreliability. The author also refutes the excuse that other companions were busy with trade.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fifth and sixth objections. Notice the shift from a brief statement by al-Bara' to a detailed argument.
2
Understand al-Bara's objection
Al-Bara' says 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' This directly accuses some narrators of fabricating.
Al-Bara' is a respected companion.
3
Analyze the sixth objection's core argument
The author argues that Abu Hurairah's narrations (over 2000) are far too many given his short companionship and average intelligence, especially compared to the senior companions who narrated little despite being with the Prophet longer.
The number 'half of the Sahihs' is an estimate.
4
Examine the refutation of an excuse
The author anticipates and rebuts the excuse that other companions were busy with trade, pointing out that Abu Bakr left trade and was still quiet.
This strengthens the criticism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم بذلك . أنبأني بهذا الخبر : الفضل بن عباس . واتفق أنه كان ميتاً في ذلك الوقت . وثانيها : أنه لما روى قوله عليه السلام : « إذا استيقظ أحدكم فلا يغمسن يده في الإناء ، حتى يغسلها ثلاثاً ، قال ابن عباس كالمنكر لقوله : فما تصنع بمهر أستا ؟ وثالثها : ما روي أن عمر رضي الله عنه منع أبا هريرة عن الرواية ، وعلاه بالدرة . فنقول : أبو هريرة إن كان صادقاً في تلك الروايات ، صار عمر مطعوناً فيه ، بسبب ذلك المنع ، وإن كان متهماً فقد صار أبو هريرة مطعوناً ورابعها : إن أبا هريرة ، كان يقول : ( حدثني خليلي أبو القاسم ، فمنعه علي بن أبي طالب . وقال : متى كان خليلا لك ؟ . وخامسها : إن البراء بن عازب، طعن في أبي هريرة . وقال : سمعنا كما سمعوا . لكنهم حدثوا بما لم يسمعوا . وسادسها : إن الأفاضل الذين صحبوا من أول المبعث إلى آخر الوفاة ما حدثوا إلا قليلا . كأبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهم - مع كثرة علومهم وقوة خواطرهم ، وشدة ملازمتهم لحضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وجـد النبي في إرشادهم وتعليمهم . وأما أبو هريرة فإنه لم يصل الى خدمة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا مـدة قليلة ، وهو في نفسه ، ما كان زائداً في الذكاء والفطنة على أبي بكر وعلي ثم إنه روى ألفي خبر ، وأكثر . وهو نصف الصحاح . وهذا يدل على الطعن الشديد . لأنه لو قدر في تلك المدة القليلة على تحصيل هذه العلوم الكثيرة ، مع أن أبا بكر وعلياً ، ما قدرا على عشر تحصيل تلك العلوم ، في المدة الطويلة ، لوجب القطع بأن أبا هريرة ، كان أفضل منهما ، وأكثر علماً منها . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ولا يقال : إن سائر الصحابة كانوا مشغولين بالتجارات ، وطلب الدنيا وأن أبا هريرة كان ملازماً للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم . لانا نقول : إن أبا بكر ترك التجارة بالكلية ، واشتغل بخدمة النبي . وفي زمان الإمامة ، كان مقدم الأمة ، والمرجوع إليه في حل المشكلات ، وإزاحة المعضلات . فكان هو أولى بالرواية . فحيث لم يفعل ذلك ، علمنا أن تركه هذه الروايات كان أولى . باتفاق أكابر الصحابة . وحينئذ يتوجه الطعن في أبي هريرة .
I know that. He informed me of this report: al-Fadl ibn Abbas. And it happened that he was dead at that time. The second [objection]: that when he narrated his saying, peace be upon him: 'When one of you wakes up, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it three times,' Ibn Abbas said, as if denying his saying: 'Then what do you do with the Mahr?' The third: what was narrated that Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating and struck him with a whip. We say: If Abu Hurairah was truthful in those narrations, then Umar becomes criticized because of that prevention, and if he was accused, then Abu Hurairah becomes criticized. The fourth: that Abu Hurairah used to say: 'My friend Abu al-Qasim told me,' so Ali ibn Abi Talib prevented him and said: 'When was he [your] friend?' The fifth: that al-Bara' ibn 'Azib criticized Abu Hurairah and said: 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' The sixth: that the virtuous ones who accompanied [the Prophet] from the beginning of the mission until the end of his death narrated only a little, like Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali - may Allah be pleased with them - with their abundance of knowledge, strength of minds, and intense adherence to the presence of the Prophet, and the Prophet's effort in guiding and teaching them. As for Abu Hurairah, he only reached the service of the Prophet for a short period, and in himself he was not superior in intelligence and acumen to Abu Bakr and Ali, then he narrated two thousand reports, and more, which is half of the Sahihs. This indicates severe criticism. Because if he was able in that short period to acquire this abundant knowledge, while Abu Bakr and Ali were not able to acquire a tenth of that knowledge in the long period, then it would be necessary to decide that Abu Hurairah was better than them and more knowledgeable than them. And it is known that that is false. And it should not be said that the rest of the Companions were busy with trades and seeking the world, and that Abu Hurairah was adherent to the Prophet. For we say: Abu Bakr left trade completely and engaged in the service of the Prophet. And during the time of leadership, he was the foremost of the nation and the one referred to in solving problems and removing difficulties. So he was more entitled to narration. Since he did not do that, we know that his leaving these narrations was better by agreement of the senior Companions. Therefore, the criticism against Abu Hurairah is directed.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections (طعون) against Abu Hurairah's narrations. The author begins with a report from al-Fadl ibn Abbas that seems to conclude a previous point. Then the second objection: Ibn Abbas appears to question a hadith about not dipping a hand after waking by asking a rhetorical question. The third: Umar prevented Abu Hurairah from narrating and struck him; the author argues either Abu Hurairah is truthful, making Umar at fault, or he is suspect, making himself at fault. The fourth: Ali ibn Abi Talib objected to Abu Hurairah calling the Prophet his friend. The fifth: al-Bara' ibn 'Azib claimed that others narrated what they didn't hear. The sixth, most detailed: the senior companions narrated little despite long companionship and intelligence, while Abu Hurairah, with short service and no superiority, narrated two thousand hadith (half of the Sahihs). This implies either Abu Hurairah is superior (false) or his narrations are unreliable. The author then refutes the excuse that others were busy with trade by noting that Abu Bakr left trade and was the foremost in knowledge and leadership, so he should have narrated more; his refraining from narrating indicates it was better, by consensus of senior companions, thus the criticism against Abu Hurairah is valid.

Key Terms
طعن
أبو هريرة
رواية
البراء بن عازب
صحاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many narrations indicate credibility and closeness to the Prophet.
Correction: The author argues that Abu Hurairah's large output, given his short companionship, actually indicates unreliability.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth objection, why is Abu Hurairah's large number of narrations considered problematic?
A) He was not intelligent.
B) He spent little time with the Prophet yet narrated more than senior companions.
C) He was known to lie.
D) The Prophet forbade him.

Key Takeaway
The quantitative disparity between Abu Hurairah and senior companions, along with a companion's accusation, forms a strong case against his reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a hadith from Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him), does any doubt about its authenticity ever cross your mind? After reading this criticism, are you more inclined to accept it or reject it? What is the basis of your stance before Allah?
?
Al-Bara' accused: 'We heard as they heard, but they narrated what they did not hear.' Have you ever spoken about Islam without being absolutely certain you have the correct knowledge? Will you commit today to verifying every piece of religious information you share, no matter its source?
?
Compare your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge with that of Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) and Abu Hurairah. How much time do you invest, and how much do you share? Does your output reflect genuine learning or mere repetition? What is one change you can make this week to prioritize quality over quantity in your religious practice?

Abu Hanifa Refutes a Qadari Through Letters
Abu Hanifa questions a Qadari about the letters of the alphabet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa used a simple question about the articulation of letters to refute the Qadari claim that humans independently create their own actions.

Core Idea
If you cannot explain how you produce sounds, how can you claim to create them?

This lesson presents a famous anecdote from the life of Imam Abu Hanifa, in which he engages a Qadari (a proponent of free will) in a debate. By asking about the mechanics of speech sounds, Abu Hanifa uncovers a powerful logical argument: human ignorance of one's own actions proves that humans are not the true creators of their deeds. Read the passage carefully to see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow of the debate.
2
Identify the key questions
Notice what Abu Hanifa asks about: the articulation of specific letters.
3
Observe the Qadari's response
He admits he does not know the points of articulation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand Abu Hanifa's logical inference: if you don't know the mechanism, you cannot be the creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الرابعة : جاء قدري ليتكلم مع أبي حنيفة . فقال أبو حنيفة : هل يمكنك التكلم بالحروف ؟ فقال نعم فقال اذكر : فذكرها . فقال : أين مخرج الحاء والخاء ؟ وكم بينهما ؟ وأي شيء تفعل حتى تخرج الحاء ؟ وأي شيء تفعل حتى تخرج الخاء ؟ فقال : لا أعرف . فقال أبو حنيفة : من لا يعرف هذه الأشياء . فكيف يمكنه أن يخلقها ويوجدها ؟
The fourth story: A Qadari came to debate with Abu Hanifa. Abu Hanifa said: Can you speak with letters? He said: Yes. He said: Mention them. So he mentioned them. He said: Where is the point of articulation of ḥā' and khā'? And how far between them? And what do you do to bring out ḥā'? And what do you do to bring out khā'? He said: I do not know. Abu Hanifa said: One who does not know these things, how can he create them and bring them into existence?
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote presenting a refutation of the Qadariyya doctrine by Imam Abu Hanifa. The argument proceeds by questioning the Qadari about the production of speech sounds (the letters ḥā' and khā'). Abu Hanifa first gets the Qadari to admit that he can speak with letters and then asks him to explain the points of articulation (makhārij) and the actions required to produce each sound. When the Qadari admits ignorance, Abu Hanifa concludes that if a person does not understand how to produce these sounds which are part of his own action, it is impossible for him to create them ex nihilo. The implied theological point is that only Allah, who has full knowledge and power, is the true creator of all actions, including human speech. This guards against the Qadari claim that humans independently create their own acts.

Key Terms
قدري
حرف
مخرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the inability to create letters, so it proves humans cannot write.
Correction: Actually, the argument focuses on the mechanism of speech sounds to show that humans lack knowledge of their own actions, implying they are not their independent creators.

Checkpoint
What was the Qadari unable to answer in Abu Hanifa's argument?
A) The number of letters in the alphabet
B) The point of articulation of specific letters
C) The meaning of the letters
D) The origin of speech

Key Takeaway
The anecdote teaches that human ignorance of the details of their own actions points to Allah as the true creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you ever pause to consider that every letter you utter is created by Allah? How would your daily conversations change if you truly believed that?
?
If you cannot even explain how you form a simple sound like ح or خ, what makes you think you are the independent creator of any of your actions? Write down one area where you tend to feel self-sufficient and confess your dependence on Allah.
?
Abu Hanifa’s argument humbles human pride in knowledge. Is there an area in your life where you assume you have full control, but in reality you are ignorant of its inner workings? Will you surrender that to Allah today?

The Paradox of Prayer: Qadari Contradiction in a Stolen Cloak
Anecdote illustrating Qadari contradiction on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple anecdote exposes the logical inconsistency in the Qadari belief that human actions are independent of God's will.

Core Idea
If God's will does not determine events, then prayer is meaningless.

This story from early Islamic theological debates captures a powerful refutation of the Qadari (Mu'tazili) position on free will. Amr ibn Ubayd, a prominent Qadari, is asked to pray for the return of a stolen cloak. His own prayer inadvertently reveals the flaw in his theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the story and its argument come through naturally.
Notice the structure: request, prayer, objection.
2
Identify Amr's statements
In his prayer, Amr makes two claims: God did not will the theft, yet it happened. And he asks God to return the cloak. Write these down.
3
Understand the man's rebuttal
The man points out the contradiction: if God's will is ineffective in preventing the theft, it is also ineffective in restoring it. Why would prayer matter?
This is the core logical refutation.
4
Connect to Qadari theology
The Qadari believe human actions are independent of God's will. This story shows that such a view makes prayer inconsistent and ultimately absurd.
Prayer presupposes God's effective will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الخامسة : جاء رجل إلى عمرو بن عبيد . وقال : أنت مستجاب الدعوة . وقد سرق ردائي. فادع الله أن يرد علي ردائي . فرفع يده وقال : اللهم إنك لم ترد أن يسرق ، وقد سرق . اللهم فرد عليه رداءه . فقال الرجل : امسك عن الدعاء. فإنه لم يرد أن يسرق ردائي وقد سرق ، فإذا أراد أن يرد علي ردائي ، لم يرده أيضاً
The fifth story: A man came to Amr ibn Ubayd and said: You are one whose supplication is answered. My cloak has been stolen. So supplicate to Allah to return my cloak to me. He raised his hand and said: O Allah, You did not will that it be stolen, yet it was stolen. O Allah, so return his cloak to him. The man said: Stop the supplication. For He did not will that my cloak be stolen yet it was stolen, so if He wills to return my cloak to me, He will not return it either.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This anecdote exposes a contradiction in the Qadari position. Amr ibn Ubayd, a Mu'tazili (Qadari) leader, is asked to pray to God for the return of a stolen cloak. In his prayer, he states that God did not will the theft to happen, yet it happened. Then he asks God to return it. The man who requested the prayer immediately points out the inconsistency: if God's will is not effective in preventing the theft, then His will is also ineffective in restoring the cloak. The argument shows that the Qadari belief in human free will apart from God's will leads to a logical problem: if God's will does not determine events, then prayer is futile. The man's conclusion is that since God did not will the theft but it happened, if God wills the return, it will also not happen because His will is not decisive. This refutes the idea that human actions are independent of divine will.

Key Terms
دعاء
إرادة
سرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story is about the efficacy of prayer or the sincerity of Amr ibn Ubayd.
Correction: The story's point is the logical consequence of the Qadari view: if God's will does not determine events, then prayer (which asks God to intervene) is futile. The focus is on the inconsistency, not on whether prayer works.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the man point out in Amr ibn Ubayd's prayer?
A) God's will is not effective in the world
B) If God did not will the theft, then He also does not will the return
C) The man should not have asked for prayer
D) Amr's prayer was not sincere

Key Takeaway
The Qadari belief that human actions are independent of God's will leads to the absurd conclusion that prayer is futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you truly believe that Allah's will governs every outcome, or do you harbor the idea that your own efforts or choices are ultimately decisive? How does this story expose that hidden assumption in your heart?
?
Think of a situation where you prayed fervently but then acted as if the result depended entirely on you. What does that reveal about your actual conviction of Allah's control? Will you repent and align your heart with the truth that His will prevails?
?
Are you willing to examine your daily life—your work, your relationships, your fears—and ask: 'Am I living as if Allah's will is real and effective, or as if I am independent of Him? Will you take one concrete step today to surrender that area to His will?'
