Refutation: Does God’s Creation of Disbelief Negate His Blessings?
Refutation of an opponent's argument that God's creation of disbelief negates His blessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that if God creates disbelief, He is not a blessing, by showing that the entailment leads to an absurd conclusion.

Core Idea
If God’s creating disbelief made Him no blessing, that conclusion itself is false.

In this passage, the author takes on an opponent's argument that seems to challenge God's attribute of being a source of blessing. The opponent claims that if God creates disbelief and destines the disbeliever for eternal punishment, then God is not a blessing at all. The author does not directly defend God’s action but instead shows that this very claim leads to a logical dead end. By breaking down the concept of blessings and using a striking analogy, he demonstrates the absurdity of the opponent's conclusion. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and the literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
The passage uses a conditional structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and the author's response
The passage starts with the opponent's claim (if God creates... then He is no blessing) and then declares it false. Notice how the author immediately labels the consequent as false.
This is a classic refutation strategy.
3
Analyze the entailment: two types of blessings
The author explains that blessings are either religious or worldly. He argues that if God creates disbelief, He cannot give religious blessings to that person. Then he shows that worldly blessings are negligible compared to eternal torment.
Look for the two ratios used.
4
Understand the analogy of poisoned food
The author uses the example of offering poisoned sweets to illustrate that a small pleasure followed by immense harm is not considered a blessing. This analogy drives the point home.
Think about why this analogy is effective.
5
Conclude with the lesson's takeaway
The author concludes that on the supposition of God creating disbelief, He would have no blessings for the disbeliever. This shows the opponent's claim is false, which is what the author intended to prove.
Reflect on how the argument works as a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع العشرون للقوم لو أن الله تعالى خلق الكفر في الكافر، وخلقه للنار. ما كان الله نعمة أصلاً . وهذا باطل فذاك باطل . بيان الملازمة : إن النعم إما دينية أو دنيوية . ولا شك أن النعم الدينية أفضل وأكمل من النعم الدنيوية . فلو قدرنا أن خالق الكفر هو الله تعالى ، لم يكن له تعالى في خلق الكافر شيء من النعم الدينية . وأما النعم الدنيوية فهي أيضاً على هذا التقدير ، يجب أن تكون معدومة. لأن منافع الإنسان في الدنيا ومضاره فيها ، إذا تقابلا كانت منافعه بالنسبة إلى مضاره كالقطرة في البحر ، ثم إذا قوبل عمر الإنسان بالأبد الذي لا نهاية له ، كان عمره بالنسبة إلى ذلك الأبد كالعدم وإذا كان كذلك ، فلو خلق الله الكفر في الكافر وعذبه عليه أبد الآباد ، كانت المنافع الحاصلة في الدنيا بالنسبة إلى هذه المضار كالقطرة بالنسبة إلى البحار التي لا نهاية لها . ومعلوم : أن مثل هذا لا يعد نعيمة ولا منفعة . ألا ترى أن تقديم الخبيص المسموم إلى الإنسان ، لا يعد نعمة . لأن اللذة الحاصلة منه قليلة بالنسبة إلى المضار الحاصلة . فكذا ههنا . فثبت : أنه لو كان حصول الكفر بخلق الله تعالى ، لم يكن الله تعالى في حق الكافر شيء من النعم البتة .
The twentieth type for the people: If God Almighty created disbelief in the disbeliever and created him for the Fire, God would not be a blessing at all. This is false, so that is false. Explanation of the entailment: Blessings are either religious or worldly. There is no doubt that religious blessings are better and more perfect than worldly blessings. If we suppose that the Creator of disbelief is God Almighty, then He Almighty would have nothing of religious blessings in creating the disbeliever. As for worldly blessings, they also, on this supposition, must be non-existent. Because the benefits of man in this world and his harms, if they are compared, his benefits relative to his harms are like a drop in the ocean. Then if man's lifespan is compared to eternity which has no end, his lifespan relative to that eternity is like non-existence. And if that is the case, if God created disbelief in the disbeliever and tormented him for it forever, the benefits obtained in this world relative to these harms are like a drop relative to endless oceans. And it is known that such is not considered a blessing or benefit. Do you not see that offering poisoned khabees to a person is not considered a blessing, because the pleasure obtained from it is small relative to the harms obtained? So here it is the same. So it is established: if the occurrence of disbelief is by God's creation, then God Almighty has nothing of blessings for the disbeliever at all.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument (attributed to a certain group) that if God creates disbelief in the disbeliever and creates him for Hell, then God is not a blessing at all. The author first states the claim and then declares it false, implying the consequent is false. The core of the argument is an entailment: blessings are only two kinds, religious and worldly. Religious blessings are superior. If God creates disbelief, He cannot give religious blessings to that disbeliever since disbelief itself is the absence of religious blessing. As for worldly blessings, they are also rendered negligible when compared to eternal punishment. The author compares the ratio of worldly benefits to harms as a drop in the ocean; then compares the finite lifespan to eternity, making it essentially nil. Hence, even if some worldly pleasures exist, they are outweighed by infinite torment, so they cannot be counted as true blessings. The analogy of poisoned food illustrates that a small pleasure followed by great harm is not a blessing. Thus, the conclusion holds: on the supposition that God creates disbelief, He would have no blessings for the disbeliever. The refutation appears to be directed against a contrary position that might argue the same, but the author is showing that the initial claim (that God is not a blessing) is absurd because it leads to an impossibility or contradicts established truths.

Key Terms
النعم الدينية
النعم الدنيوية
الخبيص المسموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is denying God's blessings altogether.
Correction: The author is not denying God's blessings; he is refuting an opponent's claim by showing that if the opponent's premise were true, then the conclusion that God is no blessing would follow, but that conclusion is false, so the premise is false.

Checkpoint
What does the author use the analogy of poisoned food (khabees) to illustrate?
A) That worldly blessings are always harmful.
B) That a small pleasure followed by great harm is not a blessing.
C) That God does not create disbelief.
D) That religious blessings are inferior.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that God's creation of disbelief negates His blessing is refuted by showing that on that supposition, the concept of blessing becomes meaningless due to the overwhelming harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that the fleeting pleasures of this world, no matter how intense, are like a drop in the ocean compared to the eternity of the hereafter? How does this realization affect the way you evaluate your daily choices? What is one indulgence you find it hard to let go of?
?
The poisoned sweets analogy is powerful: are there actions or habits in your life that bring you short-term satisfaction but could lead to long-term spiritual harm? Be honest—what is one thing you need to stop doing today?
?
If the opponent's argument were true, God would not be a blessing to the disbeliever at all. But you know God is a blessing. How does your gratitude for God's blessings—especially the gift of faith—move you to worship Him more sincerely? What is one concrete way you can show that gratitude today?

Opening of the Seventh Chapter: The Mu'tazila's Use of Reports
Chapter heading introducing the seventh topic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the seventh chapter's title and what it reveals about the Mu'tazila's engagement with transmitted reports.

Core Idea
Even rationalist theologians like the Mu'tazila used hadith to argue.

This lesson introduces the seventh chapter of al-Matalib al-'Aliyah, which turns to the Mu'tazila's use of transmitted reports (akhbar). The chapter heading signals a critical examination of how a school known for rationalism also relied on scriptural sources. Understanding this heading sets the stage for the detailed arguments to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
Read the Arabic heading and its translation. Note the key terms: al-bab al-sabi' (seventh chapter), tamasukat (arguments or clingings), al-Mu'tazila, al-akhbar (reports).
2
Understand the term 'tamasukat'
The word 'tamasukat' implies the Mu'tazila hold firmly to these reports as evidence. Reflect on why this might be surprising given their reputation.
The Mu'tazila are often labeled as rationalists who prioritize reason over revelation.
3
Consider the broader context
This chapter likely analyzes specific examples of Mu'tazilite argumentation. Ask yourself: what kinds of reports (hadith, historical accounts) might they use?
4
Check your prior assumption
Many assume the Mu'tazila rejected all hadith. This chapter heading shows they did use some reports, but perhaps selectively.
The common misunderstanding is included below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السابع في تمسكات المعتزلة بالأخبار :
The seventh chapter on the arguments of the Mu'tazila by means of reports:
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This is a chapter heading indicating that the forthcoming discussion will examine how the Mu'tazila use transmitted reports (akhbar, i.e., hadith and historical accounts) to support their theological positions. The term 'تمسكات' denotes their clinging to or argumentation using these reports. This sets up a critical analysis of the Mu'tazilite methodology regarding scriptural sources, within a work likely refuting their positions.

Key Terms

تمسكات
المعتزلة
الأخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila completely rejected all hadith and transmitted reports.
Correction: This chapter heading shows they used reports as arguments, though they may have been selective or interpreted them rationally.

Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi's Poem on Human Acts
Mu'tazilite poem classifying human acts and responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite classification of human acts into three categories based on divine involvement and how blame is assigned accordingly.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites classified actions into three categories to preserve human responsibility while limiting divine involvement in evil.

This lesson presents a succinct poem by the Mu'tazilite theologian Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi, which encapsulates the Mu'tazilite position on human acts and divine responsibility. The poem categorizes actions into three possibilities based on God's role, and then assigns blame in each case. This classification is central to the Mu'tazilite attempt to reconcile divine omnipotence with human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation, noting the three categories mentioned.
2
Identify the three categories
Identify each of the three scenarios: God alone creates, God shares, or God has no part. Notice the different outcomes for blame.
The poem uses 'صنعتها' (creates) and 'جنايتها' (perpetration).
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning
Reflect on how this classification preserves human accountability for evil acts while distancing God from direct creation of evil.
This was a key point of debate with the Ash'aris.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أبا العباس الناشي (۱) ـ من المعتزلة - نظم هذا المعنى . فقال : إن تعد أفعالنا اللاتي نلم بها إحدى ثلاث خلال في معانيها إما تفرد مولانا بصنعتها فاللوم يسقط عنا حين تأتيهــا أو كان يشركنا فاللوم يلحقه إن كان يلحقنا من لائم فيها وإن لم يكن لإلهي في جنايتها فعل فما الذنب إلا ذنب جانيها
Then Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi (1) – of the Mu'tazila – versified this meaning. He said: If our actions that we commit are among three kinds in their meanings: either our Master alone creates them, so blame is removed from us when we do them; or He shares with us, so blame attaches to Him if any blamer blames us; and if there is no act of God in their perpetration, then there is no sin except the sin of their perpetrator.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents a poem by Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi, a Mu'tazilite, summarizing the Mu'tazilite argument about human actions. It classifies human acts into three categories based on God's involvement: (1) God alone creates the act, so the human is not to blame; (2) God and the human share in the act, so blame falls on God; (3) God has no part, so only the human is guilty. This is a classic Mu'tazilite position that attempts to reconcile divine omnipotence with human responsibility by limiting God's direct involvement in evil acts.

Key Terms
الناشي
المعتزلة
أفعالنا
صنعتها
اللوم
الذنب
الجانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem supports fatalism or that God creates all actions including evil.
Correction: The poem actually denies that God creates evil actions; it argues that evil actions are caused solely by humans, and God is not responsible for them.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the case when God alone creates an action?
A) The human is blamed
B) The human is not blamed
C) Both are blamed
D) Neither is blamed

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite poem classifies human acts into three types based on divine involvement, reserving blame for humans only when God has no part in the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'God made me do this' or excusing yourself? How does this poem force you to reconsider that habit?
?
If you truly believed that God has no part in your wrongdoings, how would your repentance change starting today?
?
In what area of your life have you been shifting blame onto circumstances or others, when this passage insists the sin is entirely yours? What will you do about it before tonight?

The Dilemma of Predestination: Al-Nafs al-Zakiyya's Debate
A story of al-Nafs al-Zakiyya debating his father about predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that free will is necessary for just accountability, as illustrated by al-Nafs al-Zakiyya's dialogue with his father.

Core Idea
Justice requires free will; compulsion nullifies moral responsibility.

This lesson presents a famous debate between al-Nafs al-Zakiyya (Muhammad ibn Abdullah) and his father over predestination. The son uses a simple dilemma: either actions come from the human (so accountability is fair) or God compels them (making punishment unjust). The father is convinced and changes his position. This passage encapsulates a core Mu'tazilite proof for free will based on divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the dialogue format and the father's initial objection.
2
Identify the dilemma
Al-Nafs al-Zakiyya presents two options. Identify each and understand the logical conclusion for accountability.
3
Reflect on the outcome
The father converts after reflection. Consider why the dilemma was persuasive from a Mu'tazilite perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقلوا : أن النفس الزكية : محمد بن عبد الله بن الحسن بن علي بن أبي طالب : قبل الاعتزال عن واصل بن عطاء . فقال أبوه عبد الله يوماً : يا ولدي أنت كامل في كل شيء لولا أنك تقول بالقدر . فقال النفس الزكية : أيها الأب أتلزمني على فعلي ، أو على ما خلق الله في جبرا وقسراً ؟ فإن كنت تلزمني على فعل صدر عني ، فهذا هو مذهبي . وإن كنت تلزمني على فعل خلقه الله في جبراً وقسراً . فما ذنبي ؟ قالوا : فتفكر عبد الله في كلام ابنه ، وانتقل إلى مذهبه
They narrated that al-Nafs al-Zakiyya: Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Abi Talib: adopted the Mu'tazilite position from Wasil ibn Ata'. His father Abdullah said one day: 'O my son, you are perfect in everything except that you speak of predestination.' Al-Nafs al-Zakiyya said: 'O father, do you hold me accountable for my action, or for what God created in me by compulsion and force? If you hold me accountable for an action that originated from me, then this is my doctrine. But if you hold me accountable for an action that God created in me by compulsion and force, then what is my sin?' They said: Then Abdullah reflected on his son's words and converted to his doctrine.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage recounts a debate between al-Nafs al-Zakiyya (Muhammad b. Abdullah) and his father Abdullah, who was initially a Sunni (or non-Mu'tazilite) but later converted. Al-Nafs al-Zakiyya presents a dilemma: either actions come from the human (so accountability is just) or God compels them (so accountability is unjust). This is a classic Mu'tazilite proof for free will, arguing that divine justice requires human freedom. The father is convinced and adopts the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms

الجبر

القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story proves that the Mu'tazilite view is correct.
Correction: The author presents this as a report to be refuted later; the story itself does not establish truth, only illustrates an argument.

Checkpoint
What was the father's initial objection to his son?
A) He was too perfect
B) He spoke about predestination
C) He was a Mu'tazilite
D) He argued too much

Key Takeaway
Al-Nafs al-Zakiyya's dilemma shows that just accountability requires human free will, a foundational Mu'tazilite argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel responsible, or do you quietly excuse yourself by thinking 'it was written'? Which branch of the dilemma do you actually live by? Confess honestly: are you blaming fate for your failures?
?
If God held you accountable for an action you had no power to avoid, would that be just? How does your answer change the way you pray for forgiveness? Will you repent today for any fatalism that has robbed you of sincere repentance?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and He asks you about a sin. Would you dare to say 'You compelled me'? How does your certainty about justice and free will shape the way you treat others when they wrong you? Will you commit today to holding yourself accountable before blaming others or God?

Umar's Refutation of the Jathliq: God's Absolute Guidance and Misguidance
Report of Umar's sermon and his response to a Christian leader about guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the report of Umar's sermon provides evidence for the Sunni belief that God is the sole guide and misleader.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses both guidance and misguidance.

This lesson presents a historical report narrated by al-Qadi Abu Bakr that was used by Sunni scholars to counter the Mu'tazilite claim that God only guides. The report shows Umar ibn al-Khattab firmly asserting that God leads astray and punishes as He wills. The passage thus supports the Sunni doctrine of divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the context: Umar's sermon, the Jathliq's presence, and the exchange.
Notice the repeated phrase about guidance and misguidance.
2
Identify the key statement
Find the exact words of Umar's sermon: 'Whomever God guides, none can lead astray, and whomever God leads astray, none can guide.' This is the core assertion.
3
Understand Umar's response
Read Umar's reply to the Jathliq's objection. He directly refutes the claim that God only guides, and asserts God's role in creation, misguidance, and punishment.
This shows the Sunni position against Mu'tazilite theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن أصحابنا حكوا عن هؤلاء الأكابر ، ضد ما حكاه المعتزلة ا - فروى القاضي أبو بكر في كتاب الهداية : أن عمر بن الخطاب خطب فحمد الله وأثنى عليه وذكر في تحميله : « من يهدي (1) الله فلا مضل له ، ومن يضلل الله فلا هادي له ، والجاثليق بين يديه ، فأنكر و الجائليق (٢) ، ذلك بلسانه . فقال : ما يقول ؟ فقالوا : يزعم ؛ أن الله يهدي ولا يضل فقـال عمر: «كذب عدو الله ، بل الله خلقك ، وهو أضلك ، وهو يدخلك النار )
And know: Our companions have related concerning these eminent ones the opposite of what the Mu'tazila related. a – Al-Qadi Abu Bakr narrated in the book 'Al-Hidaya' that Umar ibn al-Khattab gave a sermon, praised God and extolled Him, and mentioned in his praising: 'Whomever God guides, none can lead astray, and whomever God leads astray, none can guide.' The Jathliq (Christian leader) was before him, and the Jathliq denied that with his tongue. He said: 'What does he say?' They said: 'He claims that God guides and does not lead astray.' So Umar said: 'The enemy of God has lied. Rather, God created you, and He led you astray, and He will enter you into the Fire.'
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents a report from Sunni scholars, transmitted by al-Qadi Abu Bakr, as evidence against the Mu'tazilite position. The report describes Umar ibn al-Khattab delivering a sermon in which he affirms that God is the sole guide and misleader. A Christian leader (Jathliq) objects, claiming that God only guides and does not lead astray. Umar strongly refutes this, asserting that God creates people, leads them astray, and punishes them in Hell. This supports the Sunni view that God's will and power encompass all things, including misguidance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God only guides and never leads astray, a position attributed to the Mu'tazila.
Correction: The Sunni position, as reflected in Umar's statement, is that God's power includes both guiding and leading astray, and that misguidance is part of divine justice.

Checkpoint

A) That God guides but does not lead astray
B) That God leads astray but does not guide
C) That God does neither
D) That God does both equally

Key Takeaway
This passage provides evidence from Umar that God is the sole guide and misleader, rebutting the Mu'tazilite view and affirming Sunni theology of divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Umar's bold declaration that God leads astray, does it provoke fear or trust in you? How does your own understanding of God's absolute control affect the way you view people who are not guided? Do you ever secretly think they could have chosen differently, or do you truly submit that guidance is entirely from God?
?
The Jathliq objected because he believed God only guides and does not misguide. In what areas of your life do you resist God's decree—perhaps expecting only ease and not hardship? Are you willing to accept that even your trials and missteps are under His will, and that He is just in all of them? How would that change your response to difficulties?
?
Imagine standing before Umar and hearing him say: 'God created you, led you astray, and will enter you into the Fire.' Does that statement make you more grateful for your own guidance? What responsibility does it place on you to call others to truth, knowing that guidance is entirely from God?

Ali's Teaching on Belief in Qadar
A report of Ali declaring that belief in qadar is essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of Ali's statement that belief in qadar, both its good and evil, is essential for being part of the Muslim community.

Core Idea
Belief in qadar (good and evil) is a defining mark of the community.

We now turn to a companion report that places belief in qadar at the very heart of communal identity. In a sermon from the pulpit of Kufa, Ali ibn Abi Talib declares that anyone who does not believe in divine predestination — both its good and its evil — is not truly from the Muslim community. This short statement carries profound theological weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the chain of transmission and the key phrase 'ليس منا'. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
The phrase 'خيره وشره' emphasizes the comprehensive nature of qadar.
2
Understand 'not from us'
Reflect on what Ali intended by saying those who deny qadar are 'not from us'. Consider whether this implies excommunication or a strong exclusion from the true path.
The common misunderstanding is that it means excommunication, but it likely means not following the true path.
3
Connect to theological debate
Recall that the Mu'tazilites denied that evil comes from God's qadar. How does Ali's statement directly refute that position?
The commentary explains this refutation.

Source Text

b – And it is also narrated from al-Sha'bi from Ali (may God be pleased with him) that he addressed the people from the pulpit of Kufa. He said: 'He who does not believe in predestination, its good and its evil, is not of us.'
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This is a brief but powerful report from Ali ibn Abi Talib, transmitted by al-Sha'bi, affirming that belief in qadar (divine predestination) – both its good and evil aspects – is a requirement of faith. Those who deny it are not considered part of the community of believers according to Ali's statement. This directly refutes the Mu'tazilite position that denies God's predestination of evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Ali's Statement on the Futility of Caution Against Destiny
Ali's poetic line affirming that caution does not avert destiny.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni belief that caution cannot change what Allah has decreed, as expressed by Ali ibn Abi Talib.

Core Idea
Caution cannot change destiny.

This short lesson examines a famous statement attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib (may God be pleased with him) before the Battle of Siffin. In a line of poetry, he declared that being cautious cannot prevent what has been destined. This serves as a powerful reminder of the Islamic belief in the inescapability of divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the original Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the concise wording.
The Arabic uses 'لن' for strong negation.
2
Understand the historical context
Ali said this when he intended to fight the people of Syria (the Battle of Siffin). Consider why he might have said it at that moment.
He was preparing for battle, which itself is a means, yet he acknowledged the limit of caution.
3
Grasp the theological implication
The statement affirms that divine decree (qadar) is absolute and cannot be altered by human precaution.
4
Address the misconception about fatalism
Some might think this encourages fatalism and inaction, but Ali himself took action. The lesson is to combine reliance on God with taking means.
This aligns with the Sunni balance of tawakkul and effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - وقد اشتهر عن علي رضي الله عنه أنه حين أراد حرب أهل الشام . قال : لن يدفع الحذار قدرا
c – And it is well-known from Ali (may God be pleased with him) that when he intended to fight the people of Syria, he said: 'Caution will not ward off destiny.'
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage reports that Ali, before the Battle of Siffin, recited a line of poetry affirming that being cautious cannot prevent what has been decreed. The comment by al-Qadi Abu Bakr (as mentioned earlier in the text) indicates this is a clear statement that caution is useless against qadar. This aligns with the Sunni belief in the inescapability of divine decree.

Key Terms
علي
الحذار
القدر
حرب أهل الشام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Caution is completely useless, so one should not take any precautions.
Correction: Ali's statement does not negate the importance of taking means; it affirms that the final outcome is decreed by Allah. Muslims are encouraged to take precautions while trusting in God's decree.

Checkpoint

A) Caution can prevent destiny
B) Caution will not ward off destiny
C) Destiny depends on caution
D) Caution and destiny are the same

Key Takeaway
Ali's statement reminds us that human caution cannot change what Allah has decreed, reinforcing the belief in qadar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face an outcome you did not want, do you truly believe that caution could not have changed it, or do you still harbor regret and 'what if' thoughts? How can you internalize this statement?
?
How does the knowledge that caution does not change destiny affect your daily decisions? Are you still working diligently while trusting Allah, or does fear drive you?

Ka'b ibn Zuhayr's Poem on Divine Decree
Ka'b ibn Zuhayr's line affirming that all that God decrees is accomplished.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ka'b ibn Zuhayr's poetic line serves as evidence for the inevitability of divine decree in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Divine decree is inevitable: whatever Allah decrees must come to pass.

Ka'b ibn Zuhayr's famous ode in praise of the Prophet (peace be upon him) contains a line that has been used by Sunni scholars to affirm the all-encompassing nature of divine decree. This short but powerful verse encapsulates the Islamic belief that nothing can hinder what Allah has ordained.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic line
Read the Arabic text of Ka'b's verse carefully. Notice the key terms 'قدر' (decree) and 'مفصول' (accomplished).
The line comes from the renowned Banat Su'ad ode.
2
Understand the context
The poet is addressing the Prophet, expressing that no matter how attached he is to friends, the decree of the Most Merciful is what ultimately prevails.
Ka'b was a companion who initially opposed Islam before reciting this poem in apology.
3
Identify the theological claim
The line explicitly states that everything Allah has decreed will definitely occur. Reflect on how this supports Sunni beliefs about qadar.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the quiz question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د - وقال كعب بن زهير في قصيدته المشهورة في مدح رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : وقال كـل خـلیـل كنـت آمـله لا الهينك ، أني عنك مشغول فقلت : خلوا سبيلي لا أبالكم فكل ما قدر الرحمن مفصول

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The poet dismissed his friends' advice, firm in the belief that Allah's decree alone decides outcomes. Think of a recent situation where you were anxious about the future—did your heart truly rest on 'كل ما قدر الرحمن مفصول', or were you relying on your own plans? Write down one specific worry you will consciously surrender to Allah today.
?

?
The poet's line leaves no room for doubt: Allah's decree is final. Is there an area of your life—health, finances, relationships—where you resist accepting Allah's decree? What would it cost you to say, 'كل ما قدر الرحمن مفصول' with full conviction, and what freedom might it bring?

Umar's Response to a Thief Excusing Himself by Qadar
Umar punishes a thief for using qadar as an excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is a grave lie to attribute one's sin to God's decree, and how Umar's double punishment illustrates the Islamic principle of human responsibility.

Core Idea
Using qadar to excuse sin is itself a sin.

In this short but powerful report, the Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab confronted a man who tried to justify his theft by saying 'God decreed it.' Umar's response was twofold: he punished the theft and also punished the lie against God. This story shows that in Islam, divine decree does not negate human accountability; rather, misusing qadar as an excuse is itself a blameworthy act.

Guided Path
1
Read the report carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the dialogue between Umar and the thief, and the double punishment ordered.
Pay attention to the thief's exact words and Umar's immediate reaction.
2
Identify the two punishments
Umar orders thirty lashes and amputation. The companion asks 'Why?' and Umar explains: the hand is cut for theft, the flogging is for lying about God.
Each punishment has a distinct reason.
3
Reflect on the lesson about qadar
Ask yourself: What does this story teach about the relationship between qadar and human responsibility? Why is claiming qadar as an excuse considered a lie against God?
This refutes the fatalist notion that qadar removes human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - وأوقع عمر رضي الله عنه عقوبتين على رجل قال هذا القول . فقد جيء إليه بسارق ، فسأله : لم سرقت ؟ فقال : قدر الله ذلك . فقال عمر : اضربوه ثلاثين سوطا ، ثم اقطعوا يده . فقيل : ولم ؟ فقال يقطع لسرقته ، ويضرب لكذبه على الله .
e – And Umar (may God be pleased with him) imposed two punishments on a man who said this statement. For a thief was brought to him, and he asked him: 'Why did you steal?' He said: 'God decreed that.' Umar said: 'Strike him thirty lashes, then cut off his hand.' It was said: 'Why?' He said: 'He is to have his hand cut off for his theft, and he is to be flogged for his lie against God.'
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This report shows Umar's practical response to those who use qadar as an excuse for sin. When a thief claims his theft was decreed by God, Umar does not accept this as a valid excuse. Instead, he punishes him twice: once for the theft (amputation of hand) and once for lying about God (thirty lashes). This demonstrates that, in Sunni Islam, qadar does not remove human responsibility; people are still accountable for their actions. The false attribution of sin to God is itself a sin.

Key Terms
عمر
سارق
قدر الله
عقوبتين
كذبه على الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadar means humans have no choice; they are forced to act, so they are not accountable.
Correction: Umar's response shows that humans are responsible for their actions; claiming qadar as an excuse is itself a sin worthy of punishment.

Checkpoint
Why did Umar flog the thief in addition to cutting off his hand?
A) For stealing
B) For lying about God
C) For disrespect
D) For not repenting

Key Takeaway
Umar's double punishment teaches that qadar does not excuse sin; using qadar to justify wrongdoing is itself a sin against God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you find yourself making excuses like 'It was my destiny' or 'Allah willed it'? How does Umar's response expose the danger of such thinking? What will you do the next time you are tempted to blame qadar for your mistakes?
?
Umar saw the thief's excuse as a lie against God. Have you ever justified a wrong action by saying 'Allah knows my heart' or 'It's just my nature'? How can you take full responsibility for your choices today, without hiding behind divine decree?
?
The thief received an extra punishment for his false attribution to Allah. Are there areas in your life where you have casually blamed Allah for your own negligence or disobedience? Will you repent and actively change that pattern starting now?

The Second Interpretation: iḍlāl as Declaring Someone Astray
The author presents the second interpretation with linguistic evidence and Mu'tazilite endorsement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of iḍlāl as naming someone astray and the linguistic evidence cited for it.

Core Idea
iḍlāl means to declare someone astray, not to create misguidance.

This lesson examines a key Mu'tazilite interpretation of the divine attribute of misguidance. Instead of understanding iḍlāl as Allah causing misguidance, they reinterpret it as a linguistic act of declaring or naming someone astray. We will look at the lexical evidence and poetic citations they use to support this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Note the structure: the author first states the interpretation, then gives linguistic evidence, then quotes poetry, and finally attributes the view to the Mu'tazilites.
The Arabic has some OCR artifacts; focus on the overall meaning.
2
Understand the core claim
The interpretation is that 'aḍalla' means 'sammā ḍāllan' (named him astray). This is a semantic claim about the verb's usage. The author supports it by analogy with 'ḥakama bi-l-kufr' meaning 'named a disbeliever'.
This redefines the action from causing misguidance to labeling someone misguided.
3
Examine the poetic evidence
Two lines of poetry are cited: one by al-Kumayt using 'akfarūnī' (declared me a disbeliever), and one by Ṭarafah using 'aḍallanī' which the author glosses as 'named me astray'. Analyze how these examples support the claimed meaning.
The poetic usage is key for lexical evidence in classical Arabic.
4
Recognize the sectarian affiliation
The author notes that a large group of Mu'tazilites adopted this interpretation. Reflect on why this interpretation might be appealing to them: it avoids attributing the creation of disbelief to Allah, preserving divine justice.
This aligns with Mu'tazilite theology of human free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتأويل الثاني : إن الأصل هو التسمية بالضلال . يقال : أضله . أي سماه ضالاً وحكم عليه به . وحكم (9) فلان على فلان بالكفر ، إذا سماه كافراً .
وانشدوا بيت و الكميت :
وظائفة قد أكفروني بحبكم وطائفة قالوا : مسيء ومذنب
وقال : طرفة ) :
وما زال شربي الراح حتى أضلني صديقي ، وحتى ساءني بعض ذلك
أراد سماني ضالاً . وهذا الوجه مما ذهب إليه جمع عظيم من المعتزلة
The second interpretation: The original meaning is naming as misguidance. It is said: 'He led him astray' meaning he named him astray and judged him as such. And so-and-so judged so-and-so as disbelief if he named him a disbeliever. And they recited the line of al-Kumayt: 'A faction has declared me a disbeliever for your love, and another faction said: he is an evil doer and a sinner.' And he said: Tarafah: 'My drinking of wine did not cease until my friend led me astray, and until some of that displeased me.' He intended: he named me astray. This is a position adopted by a great group of the Mu'tazilites.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents the second interpretation (ta'wil) of the term 'iḍlāl' when attributed to Allah. The interpretation is that 'iḍlāl' means to name someone astray or to declare them astray, not to actually cause misguidance. The author provides linguistic evidence: the verb 'aḍalla' can be used in the sense of naming someone a ḍāll (astray). He gives examples from poetry: a line by al-Kumayt where he says people declared him a disbeliever, and a line by Tarafah where he says his friend 'aḍallanī' meaning named him astray. The author then notes that this is the view of a large group of the Mu'tazilites. This interpretation avoids attributing to Allah the act of creating disbelief in the servant's heart.

Key Terms
إضلال
تسمية
ضلال
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite interpretation denies any role of Allah in misguidance; they simply re-interpret the language.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites do not deny that Allah 'leads astray' — they reinterpret the verb to mean 'declare astray' rather than 'cause to be astray', preserving divine justice while still acknowledging the Quranic wording.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites as presented in this passage, what does 'aḍalla' mean when attributed to Allah?
A) To create misguidance.
B) To name someone astray.
C) To leave someone alone.
D) To punish someone.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites reinterpret 'iḍlāl' as declaring someone astray, using lexical and poetic evidence to avoid attributing the creation of disbelief to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been declared astray by someone you trusted? How did that label affect your relationship with Allah, and how does this lesson’s distinction between causing and declaring misguidance help you re-evaluate that experience? What steps will you take to release the burden of that label?
?
The Mu'tazilites used linguistic and poetic evidence to defend their interpretation. When you encounter a difficult verse about Allah’s attributes, do you immediately reinterpret it to fit your understanding, or do you submit to the apparent meaning? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own approach to the Quran?
?
In the poetry, the friend led Tarafah astray through excessive drinking, but the author says he only 'named him astray'. Reflect on a time you called a fellow Muslim astray. Were you declaring a fact or imposing a judgment? How can you ensure that your words about others’ faith are rooted in knowledge and fear of Allah, not in personal opinion? What will you change today?

Sixth Interpretation: 'Idlaal' as Misleading from Paradise
Definition of the sixth interpretation that 'idlaal' means leading away from Paradise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite interpretation of 'idlaal' as leading away from Paradise and how it preserves God's justice.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites read 'idlaal' literally as leading away from Paradise, not creating error.

This passage presents a key Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quranic verses about God leading people astray. They argue that 'idlaal' means leading away from Paradise, not actively creating disbelief. This allows them to maintain that God's guidance is just and that humans are responsible for their own choices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the position, then the justification, then the proof text.
The passage is compact; take time to understand each part.
2
Identify the interpretation and its proponents
The sixth interpretation is that 'idlaal' means leading away from Paradise. The Mu'tazilites, especially the two al-Jubba'is, advocate this. Notice they call it a literal reading (haml 'ala al-zahir), not a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil).
3
Understand the literal reading argument
The Mu'tazilites argue that the verse says God leads people astray but does not specify from what. To make it literal, they supply the obvious object: the way to Paradise. This preserves the apparent meaning.
This differs from other interpretations that see it as metaphorically creating error.
4
Examine the proof text
The proof is from Surah al-Hajj (22:4) about Satan. The verse says 'he will lead him astray (yudilluhu) and guide him to the punishment of the Blaze.' The Mu'tazilites interpret 'yudilluhu' as 'lead him astray from the path to Paradise.' This supports their reading.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this matters: By interpreting 'idlaal' as punishment (misleading from Paradise) rather than creation of disbelief, the Mu'tazilites defend God's justice and human free will.
This is a central point in the debate about divine responsibility for error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتأويل السادس : حمل الإضلال على الإضلال عن طريق الجنة . قالت المعتزلة : وهذه في الحقيقة ليست تأويلا ، بل حملا للفظ على ظاهره . فإن الآية تدل على أنه تعالى يضلهم، وليس فيها دلالة على أنه عماذا يضلهم ؟ فنحن نحملها على أنه تعالى يضلهم عن طريق الجنة . ثم حملوا كل ما في القرآن من هذا الجنس على هذا المحمل (۲) وهو اختيار الجبائين . قال تعالى : ( كتب عليه : أنه من تولاه . فأنه يضله ، ويهديه إلى عذاب السعير " ) أي يضله عن طريق الجنة
And the sixth interpretation: carrying 'idlaal' as misleading from the way to Paradise. The Mu'tazilites said: This in reality is not an interpretation, but rather carrying the word according to its apparent meaning. For the verse indicates that He, Exalted, leads them astray, and there is no indication in it as to what He leads them astray from? So we carry it as: He, Exalted, leads them astray from the way to Paradise. Then they carried everything in the Quran of this kind on this basis. And it is the choice of the two al-Jubba'is. He said, Exalted: 'It is decreed upon him that whoever takes him as a friend, then he will lead him astray (yudilluhu) and guide him to the punishment of the Blaze' (Qur'an 22:4). i.e., he leads him astray from the way to Paradise.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth interpretation, which is favored by the Mu'tazilites and specifically the al-Jubba'i school (Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son). They argue that this is not a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) but a literal reading (haml al-lafz 'ala zahirihi). The Quran states that God 'yudilluhum' (leads them astray) without specifying from what. The Mu'tazilites specify the missing object as 'from the way to Paradise.' They apply this to all verses of this type in the Quran. The proof text is from Surah al-Hajj (22:4), where it is said of Satan that he will lead his followers astray and guide them to the punishment of the Blaze. Here 'yudilluhu' is interpreted as 'yudilluhu 'an tariq al-jannah' (he leads him astray from the path to Paradise). This interpretation allows them to maintain that God's 'idlaal' is just punishment and does not imply creating disbelief.

Key Terms
إضلال
طريق الجنة
المعتزلة
الجبائين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'idlaal' in the Quran implies God directly creates disbelief in people.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites interpret 'idlaal' as leading away from Paradise, not creating disbelief. It is a punishment, not a creation of error.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites' sixth interpretation, what is the object of 'idlaal' in the Quran?
A) From the truth
B) From the way to Paradise
C) From the straight path
D) No object; it is absolute

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites interpret 'idlaal' as leading away from Paradise, allowing them to uphold divine justice while maintaining a literal reading of the text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that seems to conflict with your understanding of God's justice, do you immediately try to explain it away or do you sit with the discomfort? What does your response reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazilites were so committed to God's justice that they insisted on a literal reading that preserved human responsibility. Are you as willing to let God's justice shape how you read His Book, or do you prefer comfortable interpretations that shift blame?
?
If you truly believed that every 'idlaal' in the Quran is a just punishment for those who choose error, would that change how you view your own sins? Would it make you more accountable, or more fearful? What would you need to change in your life right now to align with that truth?

The Seventh Interpretation: 'Idlaal' as Finding
Definition of the seventh interpretation with linguistic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh interpretation of the verb 'adalla' as meaning 'to find someone astray' (wijdan) rather than 'to cause to go astray', and the linguistic evidence supporting it.

Core Idea
The hamza in 'adalla' can mean 'to find', not just 'to cause'.

In this passage, the author presents a subtle grammatical reinterpretation of a key Quranic verb. Normally, the verb 'adalla' is understood causatively, but the seventh interpretation argues that the hamza prefix can instead indicate 'finding' someone in a state. This has significant theological implications. The author supports this with examples from classical Arabic poetry and usage, then cites exegetical works that elaborate on related verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the author's argument.
Note the grammatical terms: hamza, ta'diya, wijdan.
2
Identify the grammatical claim
The author states that the hamza in 'idlaal' is not transitive (ta'diya) but denotes finding (wijdan). Understand the difference.
This is the core of the interpretation.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
Look at the examples from 'Amr ibn Ma'di Karib's poetry and the phrase 'a'martuha'. These show the hamza indicating perception, not causation.
Consider how these examples parallel the Quranic usage.
4
Consider the theological implication
If 'yudilluhu' means 'He finds him astray', it avoids attributing the creation of disbelief to God. Reflect on why this matters.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتأويل السابع : أن [لا(1) ] تحمل الهمزة في لفظ الإضلال على التعدية ، بل على الوجدان . قال عمرو بن معدي كرب ، لبني سليم : قاتلناكم فما أجبناكم ، وهاجيناكم فما أفحمناكم ، وسألناكم فيما أبخلناكم ) : اي ما وجدناكم جبناء ولا بخلاء ولا مفحمين . ويقال : أتيت أرض قوم فأعمرتها ، أي وجدتها عامرة . (۱) سورة السجدة ، آية : ١٠ (۲) في مجمع البيان : كتب عليه : أنه من تولاه . فانه يضله ) معناه : أنه يتبع كل شيطان كتب الله على ذلك الشيطان في اللوح المحفوظ : أنه يضل من تولاه . فكيف يتبع مثله ويعدل بقوله عمن دعاه إلى الرحمة . وقيل : معناه : كتب على الشيطان : أنه من تولاء أضله الله تعالى وقيل : معناه : كتب على المجادل بالباطل : أن من أتبعه ووالاه يضله عن الدين . وفي تفسير الكشاف : ( ويتبع ) في ذلك خطوات كل شيطان ( عات . علم من حاله وظهر وتبين أنه من جعله ولياً له ، لم تثمر له ولايته إلا الإضلال عن طريق الجنة والهداية إلى النار . وما اری رؤساء أهل الأهواء والبدع والحشوية ، المتلقيين بالإمامة في دين الله ، إلا داخلين تحت كـل هذا دخولاً أولياً . بل هم أشد الشياطين إضلالاً وأقطعهم لطريق الحق ، حيث دونـوا الضلال تدويناً ، ولقنوا أشياعهم تلقيناً ... الخ . (٣) سورة الحج ، آية ٤٠ . (٤) زيادة
And the seventh interpretation: that the hamza in the word 'idlaal' is not taken as transitive (ta'diya), but rather as denoting finding (wijdan). 'Amr ibn Ma'di Karib said to Banu Sulaym: 'We fought you and did not find you cowardly (mā ajabnākum), and we satirized you and did not find you ineloquent (mā afḥamnākum), and we asked you and did not find you stingy (mā abkhalnākum).' i.e., we did not find you cowardly, nor stingy, nor ineloquent. And it is said: 'I came to a people's land and found it inhabited (a'martuhā),' i.e., I found it inhabited. (1) Surah al-Sajdah, verse 10. (2) In Majma' al-Bayan: 'It is decreed upon him that whoever takes him as a friend, then he will lead him astray' meaning: he follows every devil for whom Allah has decreed in the Preserved Tablet that he will lead astray whoever takes him as a friend. How can one follow someone like that and deviate from the words of the One who calls to mercy? And it is said: meaning: it is decreed upon the devil that whoever takes him as a friend, Allah will lead him astray. And it is said: meaning: it is decreed upon the one who argues with falsehood that whoever follows him and befriends him, he will lead him astray from the religion. And in Tafsir al-Kashshaf: 'And he follows' in that the footsteps of every rebellious devil. It is known from his state and apparent that whoever makes him a guardian, his guardianship produces nothing but misguidance from the path to Paradise and guidance to the Fire. I do not see the leaders of the people of desires, innovations, and the Hashwiyya, who are taken as imams in the religion of God, except as included under this inclusion firstly. Rather, they are the most severe devils in misguidance and the most cut off from the path of truth, as they codified misguidance and taught their followers... etc. (3) Surah al-Hajj, verse 40. (4) Addition.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh interpretation, which is a grammatical one. The hamza (prefix) in the form 'adalla' is usually understood as transitive (ta'diya), meaning 'to cause to go astray.' However, this interpretation takes the hamza as indicating 'wijdan' (finding), i.e., to find someone in a certain state. The author provides linguistic parallels from the poetry of 'Amr ibn Ma'di Karib, where 'ajabnākum' means 'we found you cowardly,' and from the verb 'a'martuha' meaning 'I found it inhabited.' This would allow the Quranic 'yudilluhu' to mean 'He finds him astray' rather than 'He causes him to stray.' The passage then includes a long quotation from the exegetical works Majma' al-Bayan and al-Kashshaf, which discuss the interpretation of the verse from Surah al-Hajj (22:4) and condemn those who mislead others, especially the leaders of innovation. This interpretation is presented as one of the possible meanings to avoid attributing the creation of disbelief to God.

Key Terms
همزة
تعدية
وجدان
إضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hamza in 'af'ala' verbs always indicates causation.
Correction: The hamza can also indicate finding or perception, as shown by Arabic usage.

Checkpoint
What is the grammatical function of the hamza in 'idlaal' according to the seventh interpretation?
A) Causative (ta'diya)
B) Denoting finding (wijdan)
C) Emphatic
D) Negative

Key Takeaway
The seventh interpretation maintains that 'adalla' means 'to find someone astray', not 'to cause to go astray', based on Arabic linguistic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God 'leads astray' in the Quran, which interpretation have you been assuming? How does this linguistic insight change your understanding of divine justice and your own accountability? Will you now read those verses differently, and what difference would that make in your daily reliance on God?
?
The poet found his opponents not cowardly or stingy. Think of a time you assumed someone's misguidance was caused by God or fate. How might the 'finding' interpretation shift your sense of responsibility toward those who are astray? Are you willing to see them as people whom you, like the poet, 'find' in a certain state, rather than as victims of divine causation?
